When Nafaqah Is Obligatory: Conditions and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Commentary on obligation of nafaqah and its starting point

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on when nafaqah becomes obligatory and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding transfer.

Core Idea
Nafaqah is due from the contract, not from submission, according to the relied-upon Hanafi view.

Nafaqah (maintenance) is a financial obligation on the husband for his wife. But when does it begin? Is it from the moment of the marriage contract, or only after the wife moves to his house? This passage clarifies the Hanafi position and presents a well-known narration from Abu Yusuf that differs. Understanding this dispute is key to applying the rules correctly, especially when the wife falls ill before transfer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its basis
Nafaqah is wajib as compensation for ihtibas (the wife being under the husband's control). Locate the sentence that connects nafaqah to ihtibas.
The text states: 'النفقة جزاء الاحتباس'
2
State the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon view is that nafaqah is obligatory from the valid marriage contract, not conditional on transfer. Find the phrase 'والفتوى على الأول' in the text.
This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
3
Recognize the opposing view
Abu Yusuf reportedly held that nafaqah is only due after the wife is transferred to the husband's house. Identify the narration attributed to him.
This is a weaker riwayah within the school.
4
Understand the practical consequence
If the wife falls ill before transfer, the mufta bihi view gives her nafaqah; Abu Yusuf's view would not. Trace this application in the text.
The text discusses the case of a sick wife not yet transferred.
5
Apply the conditions
Nafaqah is also conditional on the wife not refusing transfer without a legal right. Find the conditions where she does not become nashizah.
See the phrase: 'إذا لم يطالبها الزوج بالنقلة...'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في بيت أبيها) ، أو مرضت في بيت الزوج.
لا لناشزة خرجت من بيته بغير حق).
تجب ما لم تزف إلى منزله ، وهو راوية عن أبي يوسف له، والفتوى على الأول، وهذا كله إذا لم يطالبها الزوج بالنقلة من بيت أبيها ، أو طالبها ولم تمتنع، أو امتنعت لأخذ المهر المعجل، وإلا فإن طالبها بالنقلة وامتنعت عنها لا لحق شرعي فهي ناشزة تسقط نفقتها. كذا في «الفتح»، وغيره.
قوله : أو مرضت ؛ يعني إذا مرضت في بيت زوجها ابتداءً بعدما . نفسها تجب النفقة، وكذا إذا مرضت في بيتها ثم انتقلت إلى بيت الزوج أو بقيت في منزلها ولم تمنع نفسها عن النقلة ؛ وذلك لأنَّ النفقة جزاء الاحتباس، وهو موجود في جميع الصور. كذا في «الفتح».
قوله : لا لناشزة ؛ شروعٌ في بيان من لا تجب نفقته أو تسقط.
والنشور : لغة العصيان والمخالفة، وشرعاً : الناشزة المرأة التي تخرج من بيت زوجها بدون إذنه بغير حق شرعي، فلا تجب نفقتها إلى أن تعود وتترك النشوز وتسقط بالنشوز النفقة المفروضة الماضية لا المستدانة، يعني إذا كان لها عليه نفقة أشهر مفروضة ثم نشزت سقطت تلك النفقة ، بخلاف ما إذا أمرها القاضي بالاستدانة فاستدانت عليه، فإنّها لا تسقط. كذا في «الذخيرة» وغيرها. وقد ذكر في بعض الفتاوى سقوط المهر أيضاً بالنشوز، ولا عبرة به، فإنّه خلافُ الرواية والدراية.
قوله : خرجت من بيته ؛ الخروج أعم من أن يكون حقيقياً أو حكمياً، فيدخل فيه ما لو طلب الزوج نقلتها من بيت أبيها وامتنعت منها، وكذا ما لو كان المنزل لها فمنعته من الدخول عليها ، بخلاف ما إذا خرجت من بيت الغصب أو أبت المقام معه في سكني لا تجوز الإقامة فيه شرعاً ، أو أبت السفر مع أجنبي بعثه إليها لينقلها إليه، فإنّه تجب النفقة في هذه الصور؛ لأنّ الخروج والامتناع في هذه الصور، بحق فلم يكن نشوزاً. كما لو لم يعطها المهر المعجل ، فخرجت عن بيته ، وكذا لو أجرت نفسها لإرضاع صبي ولم تخرج، ولو مانعته الوطء وهي في منزله، وهو قادر على وطئها كرهاً، لا يكون ذلك نشوزاً. كذا في «البحر» و «السراج الوهاج» وغيرهما.
قوله : المهر المعجل ؛ أي الذي شرط أداؤه معجّلاً عند العقد، أو ما يكون معجلاً عرفاً لمثل تلك المرأة في تلك البلدة، فإنّ المعروف كالمشروط ، وأما المؤجل فإن خرجت لعدم وصوله تكون ناشزة.
قوله : ومحبوسة ؛ بالجرّ عطف على «ناشزة»، يعني المرأة التي عليها دين للغير إذا حبست بأمر القاضي بدين تقدر على إيفائه أو لا، قبل النقلة إليه أو بعدها، تسقط نفقتها ؛ لأنّ المعتبر في سقوطها هـو فـواتُ الاحتباس ، لا من جهة الزوج. كذا في «البحر»
وفي «الجوهرة النيرة» : إذا حبست بدين للزوج عليها فلها النفقة. انتهى. وكذا تجب النفقة إذا قدر على الوصول إليها في المحبس. كذا في «منح الغفار»، وفي «النهر» : قيّد بحبسها ؛ لأنّ حبسه مطلقاً غير مسقط لنفقتها.
قوله : لم تُزفّ بصيغة المجهول، صفةٌ لمريضة ؛ أي لا تجب النفقة لمريضة لم تبعث إلى بيت زوجها بعد العقد ، وهذا إذا كان مرضها مانعاً من النقلة إلى بيته ، وإن لم يوجد منها المنع. كذا في «التجنيس»، وهو مبني على أن وجوب النفقة بالتسليم، وإذ ليس فليس.
وذكر في «الفتح»، وغيره : إنّ المختار أنّ وجوب النفقة بالعقد الصحيح لا
In her father's house, or she fell ill in the husband's house. Not for a nashizah (disobedient wife) who left his house without right. (Nafaqah) is obligatory as long as she has not been transferred to his house, and this is a narration from Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, and the fatwa is on the first. And all of this is if the husband did not demand her transfer from her father's house, or he demanded and she did not refuse, or she refused to take the prompt dower (mahr mu'ajjal). Otherwise, if he demanded the transfer and she refused without a legal right, she is a nashizah and her nafaqah is dropped. Thus in al-Fath and others.
His statement: 'or she fell ill' means if she fell ill in her husband's house initially after submitting herself, nafaqah is obligatory. Similarly, if she fell ill in her own house then moved to the husband's house or remained in her house and did not prevent herself from transfer; because nafaqah is the recompense for being detained (ihtibas), and that is present in all these cases. Thus in al-Fath.
His statement: 'not for a nashizah' is a start to explaining for whom nafaqah is not obligatory or is dropped. Nushuz linguistically is disobedience and opposition; legally, a nashizah is a woman who leaves her husband's house without his permission without a legal right. Therefore, her nafaqah is not obligatory until she returns and abandons nushuz. And with nushuz, the past prescribed nafaqah is dropped, but not the borrowed (mustadana) one. That is, if she had prescribed nafaqah for months owed to her, then she became nashizah, that nafaqah is dropped. Unlike the case where the judge ordered her to borrow (spend) and she borrowed against him; then it is not dropped. Thus in al-Dhakhira and others. And some fatwas mentioned the dropping of the dower (mahr) also due to nushuz, but no attention is paid to that, for it is contrary to transmission and reasoning.
His statement: 'left his house' – leaving is general, whether actual or constructive (hukmi). Included is the case where the husband demands her transfer from her father's house and she refuses, and also if the dwelling is hers and she prevents him from entering it. Unlike if she leaves a usurped house or refuses to stay with him in a dwelling where it is not legally permissible to reside, or refuses to travel with a non-mahram whom he sent to move her to him; for nafaqah is obligatory in these cases because the leaving or refusal is with a right, so it is not nushuz. As if he did not give her the prompt dower and she left his house. Similarly, if she hires herself out to nurse a child and does not leave, and if she prevents him from intercourse while she is in his house and he is able to have intercourse with her by force, that is not nushuz. Thus in al-Bahr and al-Siraj al-Wahhaj and others.
His statement: 'the prompt dower' – that which is stipulated to be paid immediately at the contract, or what is customarily prompt for such a woman in that town; for the known is like the stipulated. As for the deferred dower, if she leaves due to its non-receipt, she is a nashizah.
His statement: 'and imprisoned' – in the genitive, conjoined to 'nashizah', meaning a woman who owes a debt to another. If she is imprisoned by judicial order for a debt, whether she can pay it or not, before or after transfer to him, her nafaqah is dropped because the considered factor in its dropping is the loss of detention (ihtibas), not from the husband's side. Thus in al-Bahr. And in al-Jawhara al-Nayyira: If she is imprisoned for a debt owed to the husband, she has nafaqah. End quote. Similarly, nafaqah is obligatory if he can reach her in the prison. Thus in Manh al-Ghaffar. And in al-Nahr: It is restricted to her imprisonment; because his imprisonment absolutely does not drop her nafaqah.
His statement: 'not yet transferred' – in the passive voice, an attribute of 'sick woman'; meaning nafaqah is not obligatory for a sick woman who has not been sent to her husband's house after the contract. This is if her sickness prevents transfer to his house, even if there is no prevention from her side. Thus in al-Tajnisa. And it is based on the fact that the obligation of nafaqah is by submission (taslim), and if not, then not.
And it is mentioned in al-Fath and others: The chosen (mukhtar) view is that the obligation of nafaqah is by the valid contract, not
Source pages p. 540

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rules of nafaqah (maintenance) for wives and the effects of nushuz (disobedience). The author begins by clarifying that nafaqah is obligatory for a wife who is in her father's house or who falls ill in the husband's house, as long as she has not been transferred to the husband's home. A narration from Abu Yusuf holds that nafaqah is due only after transfer, but the fatwa is on the first position (that it is due from the contract). The key principle is that nafaqah is compensation for the wife's being under the husband's control (ihtibas).

The term nashizah is defined legally as a woman who leaves her husband's house without his permission without a legal right. Such a woman loses her right to nafaqah, and even past due nafaqah (if not yet received) is dropped. However, if the wife had borrowed money for maintenance on the judge's order, that debt remains. The author dismisses any view that the dower is also dropped by nushuz, stating it contradicts both transmitted texts and rational reasoning.

The commentary expands on what constitutes 'leaving' (khuruj). It includes actual departure and constructive leaving, such as refusing to move from her father's house when the husband requests, or preventing him from entering her dwelling. But if the wife leaves for a valid reason—like the husband's failure to pay prompt dower, or if the dwelling is unlawful, or she refuses to travel with a non-mahram—that is not nushuz, and nafaqah remains. Even preventing intercourse while in the husband's house does not constitute nushuz if he is capable of forcible intercourse.

The passage also discusses the case of a woman imprisoned for debt. Her nafaqah is dropped because the husband does not have control over her. An exception is if the debt is owed to the husband, in which case nafaqah continues. Similarly, if the husband can access her in prison, nafaqah is due.

Finally, a sick woman who has not yet been transferred to the husband's house is not entitled to nafaqah, because the obligation is based on submission (taslim), which has not occurred. The author notes that the preferred view (al-mukhtar) is that nafaqah obligation begins with the valid contract itself, not submission, but this point is not fully elaborated here.

Key Terms
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance owed by the husband to the wife, including food, clothing, and housing, as compensation for her being under his control (ihtibas).
احتباس (ihtibas): Detention or being under the control of the husband; the basis for nafaqah obligation.
مهر معجل (mahr mu'ajjal): The prompt dower payable at the time of marriage or within a customarily short period. Failure to pay it allows the wife to leave without being nashizah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon) Hanafi position, when does nafaqah become obligatory for a wife?
A) Only after the wife is transferred to the husband's house.
B) Only after the wife submits to sexual relations.
C) From the valid marriage contract, even before transfer.
D) After the birth of a child.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi Hanafi view is that nafaqah is obligatory from the valid marriage contract, not conditional on transfer, as long as the wife has not refused transfer without a legal right.

Nafaqah — Maintenance

Nushuz: Definition, Exceptions, and Loss of Nafaqah
Commentary on nushuz definition, exceptions, and effects on nafaqah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define nushuz, identify valid exceptions that prevent nushuz, and explain the effect of nushuz on past and future nafaqah.

Core Idea
Nushuz drops both future and past nafaqah, but not borrowed debts, and valid exceptions preserve nafaqah.

Nushuz (disobedience) by a wife has serious financial consequences: she loses her right to nafaqah, including even past due amounts. But not every act of leaving or refusal is nushuz. This passage defines nushuz precisely, lists the exceptions where the wife retains her right, and clarifies that debts incurred by judicial order are not forfeited. Understanding these nuances is essential for correctly applying the rules.

Guided Path
1
Define nushuz
The passage states the linguistic and legal definition. Locate the sentence defining nashizah.
Legal definition: a woman who leaves her husband's house without his permission without a legal right.
2
Understand the general rule
A nashizah loses nafaqah. The text says 'تسقط بالنشوز النفقة المفروضة الماضية' – past prescribed nafaqah is dropped.
Future nafaqah also stops until she returns.
3
Identify exceptions
Leaving or refusing is not nushuz if done for a legal right. Find the exceptions: unpaid prompt dower, unlawful dwelling, non-mahram travel, etc.
Also: preventing intercourse in the marital home does not constitute nushuz if husband can enforce his right.
4
Differentiate between past and borrowed nafaqah
Past prescribed nafaqah is dropped, but borrowed (mustadana) nafaqah – ordered by a judge – remains a debt. Find the contrast.
The text: 'بخلاف ما إذا أمرها القاضي بالاستدانة فاستدانت عليه'.
5
Apply to the imprisonment case
A woman imprisoned for a debt to others loses nafaqah because ihtibas is lost. But if the debt is to the husband, or the husband can access her, nafaqah remains.
The passage distinguishes based on who holds the debt.

في بيت أبيها) ، أو مرضت في بيت الزوج.
لا لناشزة خرجت من بيته بغير حق).

قوله : أو مرضت ؛ يعني إذا مرضت في بيت زوجها ابتداءً بعدما . نفسها تجب النفقة، وكذا إذا مرضت في بيتها ثم انتقلت إلى بيت الزوج أو بقيت في منزلها ولم تمنع نفسها عن النقلة ؛ وذلك لأنَّ النفقة جزاء الاحتباس، وهو موجود في جميع الصور. كذا في «الفتح».
قوله : لا لناشزة ؛ شروعٌ في بيان من لا تجب نفقته أو تسقط.
والنشور : لغة العصيان والمخالفة، وشرعاً : الناشزة المرأة التي تخرج من بيت زوجها بدون إذنه بغير حق شرعي، فلا تجب نفقتها إلى أن تعود وتترك النشوز وتسقط بالنشوز النفقة المفروضة الماضية لا المستدانة، يعني إذا كان لها عليه نفقة أشهر مفروضة ثم نشزت سقطت تلك النفقة ، بخلاف ما إذا أمرها القاضي بالاستدانة فاستدانت عليه، فإنّها لا تسقط. كذا في «الذخيرة» وغيرها. وقد ذكر في بعض الفتاوى سقوط المهر أيضاً بالنشوز، ولا عبرة به، فإنّه خلافُ الرواية والدراية.
قوله : خرجت من بيته ؛ الخروج أعم من أن يكون حقيقياً أو حكمياً، فيدخل فيه ما لو طلب الزوج نقلتها من بيت أبيها وامتنعت منها، وكذا ما لو كان المنزل لها فمنعته من الدخول عليها ، بخلاف ما إذا خرجت من بيت الغصب أو أبت المقام معه في سكني لا تجوز الإقامة فيه شرعاً ، أو أبت السفر مع أجنبي بعثه إليها لينقلها إليه، فإنّه تجب النفقة في هذه الصور؛ لأنّ الخروج والامتناع في هذه الصور، بحق فلم يكن نشوزاً. كما لو لم يعطها المهر المعجل ، فخرجت عن بيته ، وكذا لو أجرت نفسها لإرضاع صبي ولم تخرج، ولو مانعته الوطء وهي في منزله، وهو قادر على وطئها كرهاً، لا يكون ذلك نشوزاً. كذا في «البحر» و «السراج الوهاج» وغيرهما.
قوله : المهر المعجل ؛ أي الذي شرط أداؤه معجّلاً عند العقد، أو ما يكون معجلاً عرفاً لمثل تلك المرأة في تلك البلدة، فإنّ المعروف كالمشروط ، وأما المؤجل فإن خرجت لعدم وصوله تكون ناشزة.

Commentary

The author begins by clarifying that nafaqah is obligatory for a wife who is in her father's house or who falls ill in the husband's house, as long as she has not been transferred to the husband's home. Key Terms
نشوز (nushuz): Disobedience by the wife towards her husband, specifically leaving the marital home without legal right or refusing transfer without valid cause.
ناشزة (nashizah): A wife who is guilty of nushuz, i.e., one who leaves her husband's house without his permission without a legal right.
مهر معجل (mahr mu'ajjal): The prompt dower payable at the time of marriage or within a customarily short period. Failure to pay it allows the wife to leave without being nashizah.
مستدانة (mustadana): Borrowed nafaqah, i.e., money the wife borrowed by judicial order for her maintenance, which becomes a debt on the husband. This is not dropped by nushuz.

Nafaqah — Maintenance
intermediate

Nafaqah for a Wife Imprisoned for Debt
Commentary on the ruling for a wife imprisoned for debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nafaqah for a wife imprisoned for debt and distinguish the three scenarios based on the cause of imprisonment and the husband's access.

Core Idea
A wife's nafaqah drops only when she is imprisoned for a third-party debt, not for a debt to her husband.

The Hanafi school bases nafaqah on the wife's availability (ihtibas). When she is imprisoned for a debt to a third party, the husband cannot access her, so the basis for nafaqah disappears. However, if the debt is to the husband or the husband can visit, nafaqah continues. This lesson unpacks these distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
Determine the act in question: the husband's obligation to provide nafaqah. Under normal circumstances, nafaqah is wajib as long as the wife is available (ihtibas).
Default: nafaqah is due.
2
Locate the principle (dalil)
The underlying principle is that nafaqah is compensation for ihtibas (the husband's keeping of the wife). Identify this causal link in the text.
The text states: 'al-mu'tabaru fi suqutiha huwa fawatu al-ihtibas'.
3
Apply the principle to the scenario of imprisonment for a third-party debt
When the wife is imprisoned by court order for a debt to a third party, the husband loses the ability to keep her (fawatu al-ihtibas) due to an external cause. Consequently, the reason for nafaqah disappears, and it drops.
This is the general rule: nafaqah drops.
4
Identify the exceptions
Exceptions occur when the imprisonment does not truly prevent ihtibas: (a) if the debt is to the husband (his own claim), (b) if the husband can access her in prison. In both cases, nafaqah remains.
The text cites al-Jawhara and al-Nahr for these exceptions.
5
Apply to a new scenario
Given a specific case (e.g., wife imprisoned for car loan to bank), determine if nafaqah is due based on the above rules.
Practice applying the distinctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَحْبُوسَةٍ بِدَيْنٍ. (قَوْلُهُ: وَمَحْبُوسَةٌ بِالْجَرِّ عَطْفًا عَلَى نَاشِزَةٍ، يَعْنِي الْمَرْأَةَ الَّتِي عَلَيْهَا دَيْنٌ لِلْغَيْرِ إِذَا حُبِسَتْ بِأَمْرِ الْقَاضِي بِدَيْنٍ تَقْدِرُ عَلَى إِيفَائِهِ أَوْ لَا، قَبْلَ النَّقْلَةِ إِلَيْهِ أَوْ بَعْدَهَا، تَسْقُطُ نَفَقَتُهَا؛ لِأَنَّ الْمُعْتَبَرَ فِي سُقُوطِهَا هُوَ فَوَاتُ الِاحْتِبَاسِ، لَا مِنْ جِهَةِ الزَّوْجِ. وَفِي الْجَوْهَرَةِ النَّيِّرَةِ: إِذَا حُبِسَتْ بِدَيْنٍ لِلزَّوْجِ عَلَيْهَا فَلَهَا النَّفَقَةُ. وَكَذَا تَجِبُ النَّفَقَةُ إِذَا قَدَرَ عَلَى الْوُصُولِ إِلَيْهَا فِي الْمَحْبِسِ. وَفِي النَّهْرِ: قَيَّدَ بِحَبْسِهَا؛ لِأَنَّ حَبْسَهُ مُطْلَقًا غَيْرُ مُسْقِطٍ لِنَفَقَتِهَا.)
And [a woman] imprisoned for debt. (His saying: 'wa mahbusatun' in genitive as an appositive to 'nashizah', meaning the woman who owes a debt to another, if she is imprisoned by order of the judge for a debt she is able to pay or not, before or after transfer to him, her nafaqah drops; because what is considered in its dropping is the loss of being kept by the husband, not from the husband's side. And in al-Jawhara al-Nayyirah: if she is imprisoned for a debt owed to the husband, she has nafaqah. Likewise, nafaqah is wajib if he can access her in prison. And in al-Nahr: he restricted it to her imprisonment; because his imprisonment absolutely does not drop her nafaqah.)
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi commentary on the ruling for a wife imprisoned for debt. The base text mentions 'a woman imprisoned for debt' as one of the cases where nafaqah is not dropped. The commentator explains that the ruling depends on the nature of the debt. If she is imprisoned for a debt owed to a third party, her nafaqah drops because the husband cannot keep her (ihtibas) due to an external cause, not her fault. However, if the debt is owed to the husband, she retains nafaqah because the husband is the one causing her imprisonment indirectly. Also, if the husband can access her in prison (i.e., he can visit and fulfill conjugal rights), nafaqah remains. The commentator quotes al-Nahr to clarify that only the wife's imprisonment (by the state) matters; the husband's own imprisonment does not drop her nafaqah. The ruling is based on the principle that nafaqah is compensation for the wife's availability (ihtibas). The passage also cites al-Jawhara al-Nayyirah for the exception of debt to husband.

Key Terms
احتباس (ihtibas): The husband's keeping of the wife in his home, making her available for marital relations.
ناشزة (nashizah): A wife who refuses to obey her husband without valid reason, thereby losing ihtibas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on nafaqah for a wife imprisoned for a debt she owes to her husband?
A) Nafaqah drops because she is not in his home.
B) Nafaqah remains because her non-availability is caused by the husband's claim.
C) Nafaqah becomes a debt on the husband.
D) She must pay the debt before receiving nafaqah.

Key Takeaway
Nafaqah is compensation for ihtibas; it drops only when ihtibas is lost due to an external cause, not when the cause is attributable to the husband.

Nafaqah for a Sick Wife Before Consummation
Commentary on nafaqah for a sick wife before consummation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi mukhtar view on nafaqah for a sick wife not yet transferred and identify the condition that makes it wajib.

Core Idea
Nafaqah is due by the marriage contract, not by physical delivery.

This lesson examines a specific case: a wife who becomes ill after the marriage contract but before moving to her husband's house. The base text of al-Wiqaya seems to deny her nafaqah, but later Hanafi authorities like al-Fath refine this. Understanding this refinement reveals a key Hanafi principle: nafaqah obligation is tied to the contract and the wife's willingness to be available, not her physical presence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The matn (base text) states that nafaqah is not wajib for a sick woman who has not been sent to her husband's house. Note the condition: illness prevents transfer.
The matn's wording is 'lam tuzaff' (not sent), implying absence of delivery.
2
Understand the commentator's critique
The sharh clarifies that this applies only if her illness prevents her from being moved. Even if she does not refuse, the matn says no nafaqah. But the commentator quotes al-Fath to revise this.
The commentator is signaling that the matn is not the final word.
3
Follow the tarjih to the mukhtar view
Al-Fath chooses the opinion that nafaqah is wajib by the valid contract, not by delivery (taslim). This means nafaqah is due for the sick woman provided she does not refuse to move when demanded.
Tarjih is based on analogy to hayd and nifas.
4
Apply the underlying principle
The key principle: tamkin al-istimta' (permission for enjoyment) suffices, not actual intercourse or physical cohabitation. As long as she is willing to be available, nafaqah is due.
This aligns with the broader Hanafi rule that nafaqah drops only with nushuz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَرِيضَةٍ لَمْ تُزَفَّ. (قَوْلُهُ: لَمْ تُزَفَّ بِصِيغَةِ الْمَجْهُولِ، صِفَةٌ لِمَرِيضَةٍ؛ أَيْ لَا تَجِبُ النَّفَقَةُ لِمَرِيضَةٍ لَمْ تُبْعَثْ إِلَى بَيْتِ زَوْجِهَا بَعْدَ الْعَقْدِ، وَهَذَا إِذَا كَانَ مَرَضُهَا مَانِعًا مِنَ النَّقْلَةِ إِلَى بَيْتِهِ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يُوجَدْ مِنْهَا الْمَنْعُ. وَذُكِرَ فِي الْفَتْحِ: إِنَّ الْمُخْتَارَ أَنَّ وُجُوبَ النَّفَقَةِ بِالْعَقْدِ الصَّحِيحِ لَا بِالتَّسْلِيمِ، فَتَجِبُ لِلْمَرِيضَةِ قَبْلَ النَّقْلَةِ أَوْ بَعْدَهَا أَمْكَنَهُ جِمَاعُهَا أَوْ لَمْ يُمْكِنْ، مَعَهَا زَوْجُهَا أَوْ لَا، بِشَرْطِ أَنْ لَا تَمْنَعَ نَفْسَهَا مِنَ النَّقْلَةِ إِذَا طَالَبَهَا، فَلَا فَرْقَ بَيْنَ الصَّحِيحَةِ وَالْمَرِيضَةِ؛ لِوُجُودِ التَّمْكِينِ مِنَ الِاسْتِمْتَاعِ كَالْحَائِضِ وَالنَّفْسَاءِ.)
And a sick woman who has not been sent [to her husband's house]. (His saying: 'lam tuzaff' in passive voice, an adjective for 'sick'; meaning nafaqah is not wajib for a sick woman who has not been sent to her husband's house after the contract. This is if her illness prevents transfer to his house, even if there is no refusal from her. And it is mentioned in al-Fath that the chosen opinion is that nafaqah becomes wajib by the valid contract, not by delivery, so it is wajib for the sick woman before or after transfer, whether he can have intercourse or not, whether her husband is with her or not, provided that she does not prevent herself from transfer when he demands it. Thus there is no difference between a healthy woman and a sick woman, because there is permission for enjoyment like a menstruating or postpartum woman.)
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling for a sick wife who has not yet moved into her husband's home. The base text suggests that nafaqah is not wajib because she cannot be transferred. However, the commentator clarifies that the relied-upon view (al-mukhtar) is that nafaqah is wajib by the valid marriage contract itself, not by actual delivery (taslim). Therefore, as long as the wife does not prevent her transfer when asked, she deserves nafaqah even if she is ill. The commentator analogizes to a menstruating or postpartum woman, who also cannot have intercourse but still receives nafaqah because they do not refuse their availability. The key condition is that the sick woman must not refuse to move when her husband requests it. This ruling aligns with the Hanafi principle that nafaqah is due from the contract, unless the wife is nashiz (disobedient).

Key Terms
تسليم (taslim): Physical delivery of the wife to the husband's home or making herself available.
تمكين الاستمتاع (tamkin al-istimta'): Giving the husband the ability to enjoy her, including sexual access.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Upon the valid marriage contract, provided she does not refuse transfer.
C) Only after she is cured.
D) Only if the husband can have intercourse with her.

Key Takeaway
Nafaqah for a sick wife not yet transferred is wajib by the marriage contract, provided she does not refuse to move when demanded, based on the principle of tamkin al-istimta'.

Nafaqah for a Wife Forcibly Taken: The Principle of Ihtibas
Commentary on nafaqah when wife is forcibly removed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nafaqah for a wife forcibly taken from her husband, and apply the principle that nafaqah is compensation for ihtibas to explain why it drops.

Core Idea
Nafaqah drops when ihtibas is lost, even if the wife is unwillingly removed.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains a key nuance in Hanafi family law: what happens to a wife's maintenance (nafaqah) when she is forcibly taken from her husband's home? The commentator clarifies the meaning of 'ghasb' here and shows how the principle of ihtibas governs the ruling. You will also encounter an intra-Hanafi difference from Abu Yusuf and learn which position is relied upon for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the forcible removal of a wife from her husband's custody (ghasb). The ruling is that her nafaqah is not wajib (drops). Confirm this from the text: 'لَا تَجِبُ نَفَقَتُهَا'.
The ruling applies to both free and slave wives.
2
Locate the underlying principle (talil)
The passage states the reason: 'لِعَدَمِ وُجُودِ الِاحْتِبَاسِ' – due to the absence of ihtibas (the husband's ability to keep her). Nafaqah is compensation for ihtibas; if ihtibas is lost, nafaqah ceases.
This is a talil (reasoning) based on the nature of nafaqah.
3
Distinguish between forcible and consensual removal
The commentary notes that if she leaves willingly (ridaha), nafaqah drops even more clearly (أظهر) because of nushuz (disobedience). In the forcible case, there is no nushuz, but the ruling still gives weight to the absence of ihtibas.
The key distinction is loss of ihtibas, not wife's intent.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf disagreed: he held that nafaqah remains because she did not consent and is not nashiz. The passage mentions this difference.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The fatwa is on dropping (الفَتْوَى عَلَى السُّقُوطِ). This is the relied-upon position for practice.
The tarjih is based on the principle in Al-Hidayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَغْصُوبَةٍ كُرْهًا. (قَوْلُهُ: مَغْصُوبَةٍ؛ لَيْسَ الْمُرَادُ بِالْغَصْبِ هَاهُنَا مَعْنَاهُ الشَّرْعِيَّ الْخَاصَّ بِالْإِمَاءِ، بَلْ الْأَخْذُ وَالْإِخْرَاجُ مِنْ عِنْدِ الزَّوْجِ بِغَيْرِ حَقٍّ، فَيَعُمُّ الْحُكْمُ الْحُرَّةَ وَالْأَمَةَ أَيْضًا، وَإِنَّمَا لَا تَجِبُ نَفَقَتُهَا لِعَدَمِ وُجُودِ الِاحْتِبَاسِ. قَوْلُهُ: كُرْهًا - بِالضَّمِّ - أَيْ جَبْرًا، قَيَّدَ بِهِ إِشَارَةً إِلَى أَنَّهُ لَوْ ذَهَبَ بِهَا رَجُلٌ بِرِضَاهَا فَسُقُوطُ النَّفَقَةِ أَظْهَرُ؛ لِوُجُودِ النُّشُوزِ، وَفِي صُورَةِ الْغَصْبِ خِلَافُ أَبِي يُوسُفَ، وَالْفَتْوَى عَلَى السُّقُوطِ.)
And a woman [forcibly] taken by force. (His saying: 'maghsubah'; the intended meaning here is not the specific legal meaning of ghasb for slave girls, but rather taking and removing her from the husband without right, so the ruling applies to both free women and slave girls. Her nafaqah is not wajib due to the absence of ihtibas. His saying: 'kurhan' - with dammah - meaning by force; he restricted it to indicate that if a man takes her with her consent, the dropping of nafaqah is more apparent due to nushuz. In the case of coercion, there is a difference from Abu Yusuf, and the fatwa is on dropping.)
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling for a wife who is forcibly removed from her husband's home (maghsubah). The term 'ghasb' here is not limited to its technical meaning (seizing property) but refers to any unlawful removal of the wife. The ruling is that nafaqah drops because the husband loses the ability to keep her (ihtibas) due to the actions of a third party. The commentator distinguishes between forcible removal (where she is unwilling) and consensual removal (where she goes willingly). In the latter case, nafaqah drops even more clearly because she becomes nashiz (disobedient). The passage notes that Imam Abu Yusuf differed on the forcible case, but the fatwa (operative ruling) is that nafaqah drops in both cases. This is based on the principle that nafaqah is compensation for ihtibas, and when ihtibas is lost, nafaqah ceases.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Forcible removal of the wife from the husband's custody, not the technical legal meaning of usurping property.
نشوز (nushuz): A wife's disobedience or refusal to fulfill marital obligations without valid excuse.
احتباس (ihtibas): The husband's ability to physically retain the wife in his home for marital relations and obedience.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Her nafaqah drops because the husband cannot keep her (ihtibas lost).
C) She only gets nafaqah if she returns within a month.
D) The husband must pay nafaqah to the kidnapper.

Key Takeaway
Nafaqah is compensation for ihtibas; when the husband loses the ability to retain his wife, even through no fault of hers, nafaqah drops according to the Hanafi fatwa.

Nafaqah for a Wife Performing Hajj: Only Residence Costs
Ruling on nafaqah during a wife's Hajj travel

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nafaqah for a wife performing Hajj and distinguish between nafaqah of residence and travel expenses.

Core Idea
A wife's nafaqah during Hajj is limited to residence costs; travel expenses are not owed.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses a critical question: when a wife travels for Hajj, does her husband still owe her full maintenance? The rule is that nafaqah is compensation for being available (ihtibas). Travel disrupts ihtibas, so only the basic residence-level nafaqah continues. However, Abu Yusuf introduces a nuance for obligatory Hajj, and the text explores the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Scope
What exactly does the wife get? The text says 'falaha nafaqatu al-hadari' – she receives nafaqah of residence, not travel expenses.
The ruling applies whether the Hajj is obligatory or supererogatory, but Abu Yusuf's view suggests a difference.
2
Understand the Basis (Illa)
Why is travel excluded? Nafaqah is owed in exchange for ihtibas (the wife being under the husband's disposal). During travel, ihtibas is absent, so only the baseline amount is due.
This is the general Hanafi principle for nafaqah.
3
Analyze the Tafsir of the Commentator
The commentator explains that 'nafaqah of hadar' means the amount that would suffice her in her town. The extra costs of travel (like rent for animals and houses) are not included.
The phrase 'la al-safar wa la al-kira ma'ahu' is clarified to mean she does not receive these extras even if the husband accompanies? Actually, the commentary leaves room for the alternative view.
4
Consider Abu Yusuf's Intra-Madhhab Ikhtilaf
Two narrations are cited: (1) Obligatory Hajj is an excuse, so she gets nafaqah of hadar. (2) The husband is ordered to go out with her and spend on her (implying full coverage).
These show flexibility within the Hanafi school.
5
Derive the Practical Consequence
If the husband accompanies her, he is responsible for all expenses. If he does not, she only gets the residence nafaqah.
The mufta bihi is the first view (hadar only), but the second view is a legitimate alternative for obligatory Hajj.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَحَاجَّةٍ لَا السَّفَرَ وَلَا الْكِرَاءَ مَعَهُ. (قَوْلُهُ: فَلَهَا نَفَقَةُ الْحَضَرِ لَا السَّفَرَ؛ أَيِ النَّفَقَةُ الَّتِي كَانَتْ تَكْفِيهِ فِي الْحَضَرِ لَا الزِّيَادَةَ الَّتِي سَيَحْتَاجُ إِلَيْهَا فِي السَّفَرِ؛ كَكِرَاءِ الدَّوَابِّ وَالْمَنَازِلِ. وَعَنْ أَبِي يُوسُفَ: إِنَّ الْحَجَّ الْفَرْضَ عُذْرٌ، فَلَهَا نَفَقَةُ الْحَضَرِ. وَعَنْهُ: إِنَّهُ يُؤْمَرُ بِالْخُرُوجِ مَعَهَا وَالْإِنْفَاقِ عَلَيْهَا.)
And a woman [going for] Hajj: no travel expenses and no rent with him. (His saying: 'She gets nafaqah of residence, not travel'; that is, the nafaqah that would suffice her in residence, not the increase needed in travel, like rent of animals and houses. And from Abu Yusuf: obligatory Hajj is an excuse, so she gets nafaqah of residence. And from him: he is ordered to go out with her and spend on her.)
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage discusses the nafaqah of a wife who goes for Hajj (pilgrimage). The base ruling is that she is entitled only to nafaqah of hadar (residence) while she is traveling, not the additional expenses of travel such as rent for transport and accommodation. However, if the husband accompanies her, she gets all nafaqah? The text says 'la al-safar wa la al-kira' ma'ahu' meaning no travel expenses or rent even if with him? Actually the phrase is ambiguous. The commentary clarifies that she gets only the basic nafaqah that would cover her needs in the town, not the extra costs. Then it mentions two narrations from Abu Yusuf: (1) If the Hajj is obligatory (fard), it is an excuse for her leaving, so she still gets nafaqah of hadar. (2) Another narration says the husband is ordered to go with her and spend on her during the journey. This indicates flexibility in the Hanafi school: while the general ruling is that nafaqah is tied to ihtibas, the necessity of Hajj may require the husband to facilitate her pilgrimage.

Key Terms
نفقة الحضر (nafaqatu al-hadari): The maintenance that would suffice for the wife's needs while residing in her home town.
كراء (kira'): Rent or hire charges for transport or accommodation during travel.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A wife's nafaqah during Hajj is limited to her usual residence costs; travel expenses are not the husband's obligation.

Nafaqah — Maintenance
intermediate
8 min
Nafaqah When Wife Is Taken by Force or Travels for Hajj
Explains when nafaqah is not due due to lack of confinement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on nafaqah in cases of abduction or Hajj travel and explain the principle of ihtibas.

Core Idea
Nafaqah is compensation for confinement – no confinement, no nafaqah.

This lesson examines two situations where the wife's nafaqah is not due: when she is forcibly taken from the home and when she travels for Hajj without her husband. The key to understanding both rulings is the legal principle that nafaqah is a recompense for the wife's confinement (ihtibas). Without confinement, the obligation drops.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage addresses a wife taken by force (maghsubah) and a wife who goes for Hajj without her husband. Both share the absence of confinement.
2
Locate the underlying principle (dalil)
The text states nafaqah is 'jaza' al-ihtibas' (recompense for confinement). This is the causal link that triggers obligation.
This is the 'illa' in Hanafi legal reasoning.
3
Apply the principle to each case
In both cases, confinement is absent: due to external force or voluntary departure. Therefore, nafaqah is not obligatory.
Note the exception: if the husband accompanies her, he still only owes the residence nafaqah, not travel costs unless he initiated the travel.
4
Note the intra-madhhab disagreement
Abu Yusuf holds that nafaqah continues for a wife taken by force, and for obligatory Hajj, but the relied-upon fatwa is on non-obligation.
The tarjih is from al-Hidayah.
5
Conclude with the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical outcome: if a wife is kidnapped, she does not accrue maintenance; if she goes for Hajj without husband, she foregoes nafaqah for that period.
This underscores the centrality of ihtibas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومغصوبة كرهاً، وحاجة لا معه، ولو كانت معه فلها نفقة الحضر لا السفر، ولا الكراء
[1] قوله : ومغصوبة ؛ ليس المراد بالغصب هاهنا معناه الشرعي الخاص بالإماء ، بل الأخذ والإخراج من عند الزوج بغير حق، فيعم الحكم الحرّة والأمة أيضاً، وإنما لا تجب نفقتها لعدم وجود الاحتباس.
[2] قوله : كرهاً ؛ - بالضم - ؛ أي جبراً ، قيد به إشارة إلى أنه لو ذهب بها رجل برضاها فسقوط النفقة أظهر ؛ لوجود النشوز، وفي صورة الغصب خلافُ أبي يوسف ، والفتوى على السقوط. كذا في «الهداية».
[3] قوله : وحاجة ؛ أي لا تجب نفقة الزوجة التي ذهبت إلى الحج، سواء كان الحج فرضاً أو نفلاً، لا مع زوجها سواء كان ذهابها مع محرم أو بدون محرم ؛ لأن النفقة جزاء الاحتباس، وقد فقد هاهنا. وعن أبي يوسف له : إنّ الحجّ الفرض عذر، فلها نفقة الحضر، وعنه : إنه يؤمر بالخروج معها والإنفاق عليها. كذا في «الذخيرة».
[4] قوله : فلها نفقة الحضر ؛ أي النفقة التي كانت تكفيه في الحضر لا الزيادة التي سيحتاج إليها في السفر ؛ ككراء الدواب والمنازل، وكذا لو خرجت معه لعمرة، أو تجارة، أو نحو ذلك، وهذا كله إذا خرج معها لأجلها، أما لو أخرجها هو يلزمه جميع ذلك. كذا في «البحر»، وغيره.
And a woman taken by force, and a woman who goes for Hajj without her husband, and if she is with him, she gets nafaqah of residence [when at home], not of travel, and no rent. [1] His saying: 'And a woman taken by force' — what is meant here is not the specific legal meaning of ghasb for slave women, but taking and removing from the husband without right, so the ruling includes both free women and slave women. Rather, nafaqah is not obligatory for her due to the absence of confinement. [2] His saying: 'by force' — with a damma, i.e., forcibly. This qualifier indicates that if a man takes her with her consent, the non-obligation of nafaqah is more apparent due to the presence of nushuz. In the case of ghasb there is a disagreement from Abu Yusuf, and the fatwa is on non-obligation. [3] His saying: 'and a woman who goes for Hajj' — i.e., nafaqah is not obligatory for the wife who goes for Hajj, whether it is obligatory or voluntary, without her husband, whether she goes with a mahram or without a mahram, because nafaqah is the recompense for confinement, and it is absent here. And from Abu Yusuf: obligatory Hajj is an excuse, so she gets nafaqah of residence. And from him: he is ordered to go out with her and spend on her. [4] His saying: 'so she gets nafaqah of residence' — i.e., the nafaqah that would suffice her in residence, not the increase needed for travel, such as rent for animals and lodgings. Similarly, if she goes out with him for umrah, or trade, or the like. All this is if he goes out with her for her sake; but if he takes her out, he is obliged for all of that.
Source pages p. 542

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof from the commentary on al-Wiqayah. It addresses two cases where the wife's nafaqah is not obligatory: (1) when she is taken from the husband by force (ghasb), and (2) when she travels for Hajj without her husband. The underlying principle is that nafaqah is compensation for the wife's confinement (ihtibas) in the husband's home. If she is not confined due to an external cause not attributable to the husband, nafaqah ceases.

For the first case: 'ghasb' here does not refer to the technical legal term for seizing property, but to any forceful removal of the wife from the husband's custody without right. The qualifier 'karhan' (by force) is crucial: if she leaves with her consent, nafaqah is even more clearly not due, as that constitutes nushuz (disobedience). There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that nafaqah continues, but the fatwa is on non-obligation.

For the second case: if the wife goes for Hajj without her husband, whether obligatory or voluntary, nafaqah is not due because confinement is absent. Abu Yusuf distinguishes: if the Hajj is obligatory (fard), it is an excuse, so she receives nafaqah of residence (but not travel expenses). Another narration from him says the husband should accompany her and spend.

If the wife travels with her husband for her own purpose (Hajj, umrah, trade), she gets only the nafaqah she would have at home, not additional travel costs. However, if the husband takes her out for his own purpose, all expenses are on him.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Here it means forceful removal of the wife from the husband's custody without right, not the technical seizure of property.
احتباس (ihtibas): Confinement of the wife in the husband's home, which is the basis for nafaqah.
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance owed by husband to wife, including food, clothing, and shelter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why does nafaqah not become obligatory when the wife is taken by force?
A) Because the marriage is temporarily suspended.
B) Because nafaqah is compensation for confinement, and confinement is absent.
C) Because the husband is not wealthy enough.
D) Because the wife committed nushuz.

Key Takeaway
Nafaqah is not automatically due by virtue of marriage; it requires the wife's confinement in the husband's home.

Nafaqah of a Servant: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Number Required
Ruling on nafaqah of a servant with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on providing a servant for the wife, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding the number, and apply the condition of the wife's ownership to determine the obligation.

Core Idea
A husband must provide one servant for his wife only if she owns a slave; otherwise, he buys her necessities.

This lesson examines a specific element of nafaqah: providing a servant (khādim) for the wife. The Hanafi school requires a wealthy husband to provide a servant, but an intra-Hanafi dispute arises over how many. The passage clarifies the condition of ownership and resolves the disagreement, showing how custom and the wife's status may influence the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
Read the matn and commentary to determine the basic ruling: a wealthy husband (mūsir) must provide nafaqah for one servant. Note the condition: the servant must be owned by the wife (zahir al-riwaya).
If the wife does not own a slave, the obligation shifts to buying her necessities from the market.
2
State the default Hanafi position
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that one servant suffices for both indoor and outdoor tasks.
This is the mufta bihi position according to the majority.
3
Identify Abu Yusuf's differing view
Abu Yusuf initially required two servants: one for internal tasks and one for external tasks. However, the well-known narration from him agrees with the two imams.
Other narrations exist for noble women, possibly requiring more servants.
4
Analyze the tarjih (preference)
Scholars like al-Ayni and al-Tahawi state that the well-known view of Abu Yusuf is the same as Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, so the operative ruling is one servant.
Custom and the wife's social standing may still affect the practical application.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The dispute affects how many servants the husband must fund. In practice, if the wife is from a noble family with many servants, the husband may be required to provide more.
This shows how custom (‘urf) can modify the default ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعليه موسرا نفقة خادم واحدٍ لها فقط ) ) ، هذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد ، وأما عند أبي يوسف " فعليه نفقة خادمين أحدهما لمصالح الداخل، والآخر لمصالح خارج البيت، وهما يقولان : أن الواحد يقوم بهما
[1] قوله : نفقة خادم ؛ قال في «البحر»: «قيل : هو كلُّ مَن يخدمها حراً كان أو عبداً لمالكها أو لـه أو لهما أو لغيرهما، وظاهرُ الراوية عن أصحابنا الثلاثة كما في «الذخيرة»: إنه مملوكها، فلو لم يكن لها خادمٌ لا يفرض عليه نفقة خادم ؛ لأنها بسبب الملك ، فإذا لم يكن في ملكها لا تلزمه". وبهذا عُلم أنه إذا لم يكن لها خادم مملوك لا يلزمه كراء غلام يخدمها ، لكن يلزمه أن يشتري لها ما تحتاجه من السوق، كما صرّح به في «السراجية»، وذكر الخير الرملي : أنها إذا مرضت وجب عليها إخدامها ، ولو كانت أمة، وبه صرّح الشافعية، وهو مقتضى قواعد أصحابنا.
[2] قوله : أما عند أبي يوسف ...الخ ؛ هكذا ذكر في «الهداية»، وقال العيني في شرحها «البناية»: «هذا الذي ذكروه عن أبي يوسف الله غير المشهور عنه ؛ لأنَّ المشهور من قوله كقولهما، وبه صرَّحَ الطحاوي في «مختصره»(4). وفي فتاوى أهل سمرقند» : إذا كانت المرأة من بنات الأشراف وذوي الأقدار لها خدم كثيرة يجبر على نفقة خادمين أحدهما للخدمة، والآخر: للرسالة، وعن أبي يوسف له في رواية أخرى : إذا كانت لها خدم كثيرة وزفت إليه كذلك استحقت نفقته الخدم كلّها ، وهو رواية هشام عنه الله له اختارها الطحاوي) ().
And upon a wealthy husband is the nafaqah of one servant only for her. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. As for Abu Yusuf, upon him is the nafaqah of two servants: one for internal matters and the other for external matters of the house. They (the two) say that one suffices for both. [1] His saying: 'nafaqah of a servant' — Al-Bahr says: 'It is said that it is anyone who serves her, free or slave, belonging to her, to him, to both, or to others. But the apparent narration from our three imams, as in al-Dhakhira, is that it is her slave. So if she does not have a servant, he is not required to provide the nafaqah of a servant, because it is due to ownership; if she does not own, it is not obligatory on him.' From this it is known that if she does not own a slave, he is not required to rent a servant to serve her, but he must buy for her what she needs from the market, as stated in al-Sirajiyyah. Al-Khayr al-Ramli mentioned that if she becomes ill, it is obligatory on him to provide a servant for her, even if she is a slave; the Shafi'is explicitly stated this, and it is in accordance with the principles of our school. [2] His saying: 'As for Abu Yusuf...' — thus it is mentioned in al-Hidayah. Al-Ayni in his commentary al-Binayah says: 'What they have mentioned from Abu Yusuf is not the well-known from him; indeed, the well-known from him is like their (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's) saying, and al-Tahawi explicitly stated this in his Mukhtasar.' In Fatawa Ahl Samarqand: If the woman is from noble families and has many servants, he is compelled to provide the nafaqah of two servants: one for service and one for errands. And from Abu Yusuf in another narration: If she has many servants and was married with that status, she deserves the nafaqah of all of them. This is a narration from Hisham from him, and al-Tahawi chose it.
Source pages p. 543

Commentary
This passage addresses the obligation of a wealthy husband to provide a servant (khādim) for his wife. The basic ruling from the matn is that he must provide one servant for her only. This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Abu Yusuf differs, requiring two servants: one for internal household tasks and one for external tasks. The two imams argue that one person can handle both.

The commentary clarifies that the servant must be the wife's property (mamlūk). If she does not own a servant, the husband is not obligated to provide a rented servant; instead, he must buy her necessities from the market. However, al-Khayr al-Ramli states that if the wife is ill, it is obligatory to provide a servant even if she is a slave, which aligns with Shafi'i school and the principles of the Hanafi school.

Regarding the dispute, al-Ayni notes that the well-known position of Abu Yusuf is actually the same as the two imams (one servant). Other narrations from Abu Yusuf vary: for noble women with many servants, he may be required to provide two or even all of them if that was her status at marriage. The fatwa in practice may consider custom and need.

Key Terms
خادم (khādim): A person (free or slave) who serves the wife. In the context of nafaqah, it is specifically a slave owned by the wife according to the apparent narration.
موسر (mūsir): A husband who is wealthy, i.e., possesses the financial ability to provide a servant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) of the Hanafi school, when is a husband obligated to provide a servant (khādim) for his wife?
A) Whenever the wife requests one.
B) Only if the wife owns a slave.
C) Always, regardless of the wife's wealth.
D) Only if the wife is ill.

Key Takeaway
The husband must provide one servant for the wife only if she owns a slave; otherwise, he buys her necessities; custom may increase the number.

Obligation of Servant: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute and Conditions
Expands the rule on servant maintenance and Abu Yusuf's disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the number of servants owed and identify the conditions that trigger the obligation to provide a servant.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require one servant; Abu Yusuf requires two, but his well-known view agrees with the majority.

The maintenance of a servant for the wife is a debated component of nafaqah within the Hanafi school. While the majority holds that one servant suffices, Imam Abu Yusuf argued for two. The passage also clarifies when the obligation applies: only if the wife owns a slave. This lesson unpacks the dispute and the underlying conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that one servant suffices; Abu Yusuf holds that two are required (one for internal affairs, one for external).
Also note the third narration from Abu Yusuf requiring all servants for noble women.
2
Examine the evidence
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue that one servant can handle both internal and external tasks, including trade. Abu Yusuf sees a need for separation of roles.
The text does not provide a detailed proof, only the rationale.
3
Apply tarjih
The mufta bihi is one servant, as the well-known view of Abu Yusuf (via al-Tahawi and al-'Ayni) aligns with the majority.
This is an example of resolving ikhtilaf by preferring the well-known narration.
4
Define the servant (khādim)
According to the apparent narration, khādim means the wife's owned slave. It does not include free hired servants.
This conditions the obligation on ownership.
5
Identify conditions and exceptions
Obligation requires husband's wealth (mūsir) and wife's ownership of a servant; if no owned servant, he only buys necessities. Exception: if wife falls ill, he must provide a servant even if she is a slave.
The Shafi'i school agrees on the illness exception, consistent with Hanafi rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعليه موسرا نفقة خادم واحد لها فقط ) ) ، هذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد ، وأما عند أبي يوسف " فعليه نفقة خادمين أحدهما لمصالح الداخل، والآخر لمصالح خارج البيت، وهما يقولان : أن الواحد يقوم بهما تجارة، أو نحو ذلك، وهذا كله إذا خرج معها لأجلها، أما لو أخرجها هو يلزمه جميع ذلك. كذا في «البحر»، وغيره. [١]قوله : نفقة خادم ؛ قال في «البحر»: «قيل : هو كلُّ مَن يخدمها حراً كان أو عبداً لمالكها أو لـه أو لهما أو لغيرهما، وظاهرُ الراوية عن أصحابنا الثلاثة كما في «الذخيرة»: إنه مملوكها، فلو لم يكن لها خادمٌ لا يفرض عليه نفقة خادم ؛ لأنها بسبب الملك ، فإذا لم يكن في ملكها لا تلزمه". وبهذا عُلم أنه إذا لم يكن لها خادم مملوك لا يلزمه كراء غلام يخدمها ، لكن يلزمه أن يشتري لها ما تحتاجه من السوق، كما صرّح به في «السراجية»، وذكر الخير الرملي : أنها إذا مرضت وجب عليها إخدامها ، ولو كانت أمة، وبه صرّح الشافعية، وهو مقتضى قواعد أصحابنا. [٢]قوله : أما عند أبي يوسف ...الخ ؛ هكذا ذكر في «الهداية»، وقال العيني في شرحها «البناية»: «هذا الذي ذكروه عن أبي يوسف غير المشهور عنه ؛ لأنَّ المشهور من قوله كقولهما، وبه صرَّحَ الطحاوي في «مختصره». وفي فتاوى أهل سمرقند» : إذا كانت المرأة من بنات الأشراف وذوي الأقدار لها خدم كثيرة يجبر على نفقة خادمين أحدهما للخدمة، والآخر: للرسالة، وعن أبي يوسف له في رواية أخرى : إذا كانت لها خدم كثيرة وزفت إليه كذلك استحقت نفقته الخدم كلّها ، وهو رواية هشام عنه اختارها الطحاوي).
And upon him, if wealthy, is the maintenance of one servant for her only. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. As for Abu Yusuf, upon him is the maintenance of two servants: one for internal affairs and the other for external affairs. They (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) say that one suffices for both, trade, or the like. And all this is if she went out with him for her own sake; but if he took her out, he is liable for all of that. So in al-Bahr, etc. [1] His statement: Maintenance of a servant. He said in al-Bahr: 'It is said: it is everyone who serves her, free or slave, owned by her or by him or by both or by others. And the apparent narration from our three imams, as in al-Dhakhira, is that it is her slave. So if she has no servant, he is not obligated to provide a servant's maintenance because it is due to ownership; thus if she does not own one, it is not required of him.' So it is learned that if she has no owned servant, he is not required to hire a servant for her, but he must buy for her what she needs from the market, as stated in al-Sirajiyya. And al-Khayr al-Ramli mentioned that if she falls ill, it is obligatory to provide her a servant, even if she is a slave-woman; the Shafi'is explicitly state this, and it is in line with the rules of our madhhab. [2] His statement: As for Abu Yusuf... etc. This is mentioned in al-Hidaya, and al-'Ayni in his commentary al-Binaya said: 'What they have narrated from Abu Yusuf is not the well-known from him because the well-known from him is like their view, and al-Tahawi explicitly states this in his Mukhtasar.' In Fatawa Ahl Samarqand: if the woman is from among the daughters of nobles and people of status, having many servants, he is compelled to provide maintenance for two servants: one for service and the other for messenger duties. And from Abu Yusuf in another narration: if she has many servants and is brought to him [in marriage] likewise, she is entitled to maintenance for all the servants. This is the narration of Hisham from him, which al-Tahawi preferred.
Source pages p. 543

Commentary
This passage expands the ruling on a husband's obligation to provide a servant for his wife. The core ruling from the matn is that a wealthy husband must provide one servant. The commentator explains that this is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad. However, Imam Abu Yusuf differs, requiring two servants: one for internal household tasks and one for external errands. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue that one servant can handle both. The passage then discusses the definition of 'servant' (khādim): according to the apparent narration from the three imams, a servant is specifically the wife's owned slave. If she owns no slave, the husband is not forced to hire one; he only needs to buy her necessities from the market. However, if she is ill, she must be provided a servant even if she is a slave herself. Finally, the commentary notes a difference in Abu Yusuf's well-known position as recorded by al-Tahawi, aligning with the majority view. In fatwa, for noble women with many servants, he may be compelled to provide two servants, and in another narration from Hisham, all servants must be maintained.

Key Terms
نفقة خادم (nafaqat khādim): The maintenance paid by the husband to provide a servant for the wife, including the servant's food, clothing, and other needs.
خادم (khādim): A servant who serves the wife; in this context, it is specifically her owned slave, according to the apparent narration.

Servant Maintenance for the Indigent Husband
Clarifies the rule when the husband is poor: no servant, no separation, wife borrows.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for servant maintenance when the husband is poor, identify the wealth threshold used, and explain the wife's recourse to borrowing.

Core Idea
For a poor husband, servant maintenance is not obligatory; the wife borrows with the judge's permission — no separation.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains a key nuance in spousal maintenance: the obligation of providing a servant is conditional on the husband's wealth. Here we learn the definition of wealth in this context, the school's reliance on the nisab that prohibits charity (200 dirhams surplus), and the practical solution for the wife when her husband cannot provide: borrowing under judicial authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is the husband's obligation to provide a servant (nafaqat khādim) for his wife. The ruling is that it is NOT obligatory (ghayr wajib) on a poor husband.
The text says 'lā mu‘siran' (not for the poor husband).
2
Determine the key condition: wealth
The obligation depends on the husband being wealthy (mūsir). Wealth is defined as owning productive surplus beyond basic needs equivalent to 200 dirhams – the nisab that prohibits receiving charity (nisab ḥirmān al-ṣadaqa), not the zakat nisab.
This threshold is the same as for sadaqat al-fiṭr and al-aḍḥiyah.
3
Classify the husband as poor (mu‘sir)
If the husband does not have this wealth, he is mu‘sir, and the servant maintenance is not wajib on him. The text adopts this as the most correct view (al-aṣaḥḥ).
Imam Muhammad disagrees, holding it wajib even on the poor.
4
Apply the consequence: no separation
The court does not separate the couple due to the husband's inability to provide a servant. This applies whether he is absent or present.
If he were able but refused, the judge would sell his property or imprison him.
5
Outline the wife's remedy: borrowing with judicial order
The wife is ordered by the judge to borrow (istidānah) for her expenses. This order is essential: without it, the creditor cannot claim repayment from the husband. With the order, the debt is imposed on the husband because the judge has general authority over him.
The wife cannot impose a debt on her husband on her own; only the judge can.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا معسراً في الأصح (٢) ، احتراز عن قول محمد له، فإنَّ عنده تَجِبُ على المعسر نفقة الخادم. (ولا يفرق بينهما لعجزه عنها ، وتؤمر بالاستدانة عليه). [ ١ ] قوله : لا معسراً ؛ أي لا تجب على الزوج نفقة خادمها إذا كان محتاجاً ، والعسر واليسر هاهنا مقدّر بنصاب حرمان الصدقة لا بنصاب وجوب الزكاة، وهو المال النامي الفاضل عن حاجته الأصلية، والغنى الذي تجب به صدقة الفطر والأضحية، وتحرم به على صاحبه أخذ الصدقات : هو أن يملك ما يساوي مئتي درهم، فاضلا عن حاجته. كذا في «البناية». [٢]قوله : ولا يفرّق ؛ سواء كان الزوج غائباً أو حاضراً. [٣] قوله : لعجزه ؛ هذا ليس بقيد احترازي بل ذكره لكونه مختلفا فيه ؛ ولأنه يعلم منه شدة حكم ما إذا لم يعجز بالطريق الأولى، فإنّه إذا لم يجز التفريق لعجزه مع الاحتياج لم يجز عند القدرة عليه بالطريق الأولى، فإذا كان قادرا عليه وامتنع عن الإنفاق عليها يبيع الحاكم ماله عليه ويصرفه في نفقتها، فإن لم يجد ماله حبسه حتى ينفق عليها. كذا في «الفتح». (٤) قوله : وتؤمر ؛ أي من جانب القاضي، وفائدة ذلك : أن يتمكن صاحب الدين على أن يأخذ دينه من الزوج، فإنّ المرأة لو استدانت على الزوج بدون أمر القاضي ليس لرب الدين أن يرجع عليه، بل يطالبها بدينه ويأخذه منها، ثمّ هي ترجعُ على زوجها بما فرض لها القاضي، وهذا لأنَّ الاستدانة على الزوج إيجاب الدين عليه ، وليس لها ولاية عليه حتى يوجب ديناً عليه ، فيضم إلى ذلك أمر القاضي الذي له ولايةٌ عامة. كذا في «تحفة الفقهاء»، و«البناية». [0]قوله : بالاستدانة... ؛ أي أن تنفق على نفسها بأن يأخذ من الغير بالقرض.
Not for the poor husband in the most correct view, as an exception to the statement of Muhammad , for according to him the maintenance of a servant is obligatory on the poor husband. And they are not separated due to his inability to provide it, and she is ordered to borrow against him. [1] His statement: Not for the poor husband; i.e., the husband is not obligated to provide servant maintenance for her if he is in need. Wealth and poverty here are measured by the threshold for prohibition of charity (nisāb ḥirmān al-ṣadaqa), not by the threshold for the obligation of zakāt. It is productive wealth surplus to his basic needs. The wealth that makes zakāt al-fiṭr and al-aḍḥiyah obligatory and prohibits the recipient from taking charity is that he possesses wealth equivalent to two hundred dirhams surplus to his needs. So in al-Bināya. [2] His statement: And they are not separated; whether the husband is absent or present. [3] His statement: Due to his inability; this is not a restrictive condition but is mentioned because it is a point of disagreement; and because it implies by primary reasoning the severity of the ruling when he is not unable. For if separation is not permitted when he is unable despite need, then a fortiori it is not permitted when he is able. So if he is able and refrains from spending on her, the judge sells his property and spends it on her maintenance; if he finds no property, he imprisons him until he spends on her. So in al-Fatḥ. (4) His statement: And she is ordered; i.e., by the judge. The benefit of this is that the creditor can then claim his debt from the husband. If the wife borrowed without the judge's order, the creditor cannot claim from the husband; instead he demands his debt from her and takes it from her, and then she claims from her husband what the judge has fixed. This is because borrowing against the husband imposes a debt on him, and she has no authority over him to impose a debt, so the judge's order is added, who has general authority. So in Tuḥfat al-Fuqahā' and al-Bināya. [0] His statement: By borrowing; i.e., she spends on herself by taking a loan from others.
Source pages p. 544

Commentary
This passage addresses the situation of a husband who is too poor to provide a servant for his wife. The matn states that the obligation of a servant does not apply to an indigent husband (mu‘sir), and the commentary clarifies that this is the most correct view (al-aṣaḥḥ), distinguishing it from the position of Imam Muhammad who holds that even a poor husband must provide a servant. The commentator then defines 'wealth' (yusr) and 'poverty' (‘usr) in this context: the threshold is the nisab that prohibits a person from receiving charity (nisab ḥirmān al-ṣadaqa), which is 200 dirhams surplus to basic needs—not the nisab for zakāt. This is the same standard as for sadaqat al-fitr and udḥiyah. The passage further explains that if the husband is unable to provide a servant, the couple should not be judicially separated. This ruling applies whether the husband is absent or present. The inability is not a restrictive condition; rather it is mentioned because it is a point of disagreement with Muhammad. By primary analogy, if separation is not allowed when he is unable, it is even less so when he is able; in the latter case, the judge sells his property or imprisons him until he provides maintenance. Finally, the wife is ordered by the judge to borrow for her expenses. The significance of this order is that without the judge's authorization, the creditor cannot claim repayment from the husband; with the order, the creditor can directly claim from the husband. This is because the wife has no authority to impose a debt on her husband, but the judge's general authority enables this.

Key Terms
معسر (mu‘sir): A husband who is poor, i.e., does not possess the nisab of wealth (200 dirhams surplus to basic needs) that prohibits receiving charity.
استدانة (istidānah): Borrowing by the wife from a third party, ordered by the judge, to spend on her maintenance when the husband cannot provide.
الأصح (al-aṣaḥḥ): The most correct view, used to indicate the relied-upon position among variant opinions within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the correct measure of wealth (ghinā) used in determining whether the husband must provide a servant?
A) The nisab of zakāt (200 dirhams of silver, including trading and non-trading assets)
B) The nisab that prohibits receiving charity (200 dirhams surplus to basic needs)
C) Ownership of a house and furniture
D) Any amount above one's daily expenses

Key Takeaway
For a poor husband (mu‘sir) with less than 200 dirhams surplus, servant maintenance is not obligatory; the wife borrows with the judge's permission and separation is not granted.

Nafaqah — Maintenance
intermediate

Servant Maintenance for a Poor Husband: Hanafi Ruling
Ruling on servant maintenance and definition of mu'sir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the obligation of providing a servant for the wife when the husband is poor, and identify the definition of 'poor' (mu‘sir) used in this context.

Core Idea
A poor husband is not obligated to provide a servant; the most sound Hanafi opinion follows Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

The obligation to provide a servant (khādim) for the wife is a detail within the broader law of maintenance (nafaqah). In the Hanafi school, this obligation depends on the husband's financial status. The passage clarifies that a poor husband is exempt, and it explains how 'poverty' is measured differently here than for zakat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its general obligation
Maintenance of a servant is generally wajib on the husband for his wife's needs, but the passage examines the exception for a poor husband.
2
State the Hanafi ruling for a poor husband
The relied-upon position (zahir al-riwayah) is that a poor husband is not obligated to provide a servant. This is called 'al-aṣaḥḥ' (most sound).
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that even a poor husband must provide a servant. Know that his view is marjuh (weaker) in the school.
The author explicitly rejects Muhammad's view by stating 'لا معسراً في الأصح'.
4
Define 'poor' (mu‘sir) for this ruling
Poverty is measured by the nisab of prohibition of charity: owning less than 200 dirhams surplus to basic needs (mal namī fāḍil). This is different from the zakat threshold.
See al-Bināyah for the definition.
5
Distinguish from other definitions
The threshold for sadaqat al-fitr and udhiyah is also 200 dirhams surplus, but the key point is that nafaqah poverty uses the charity prohibition nisab, not the zakat nisab.

لا معسراً في الأصح ، ولا يفرق بينهما لعجزه عنها، وتؤمر بالاستدانة عليه (لا) معسراً في الأصح) () ، احتراز عن قول محمد له، فإنَّ عنده تَجِبُ على المعسر نفقة الخادم. (ولا يفرق بينهما لعجزه عنها ، وتؤمر بالاستدانة عليه). 
[ ١ ] قوله : لا معسراً ؛ أي لا تجب على الزوج نفقة خادمها إذا كان محتاجاً ، والعسر واليسر هاهنا مقدّر بنصاب حرمان الصدقة لا بنصاب وجوب الزكاة، وهو المال النامي الفاضل عن حاجته الأصلية، والغنى الذي تجب به صدقة الفطر والأضحية، وتحرم به على صاحبه أخذ الصدقات : هو أن يملك ما يساوي مئتي درهم، فاضلا عن حاجته. كذا في «البناية». 
[ ٢ ] قوله : ولا يفرّق ؛ سواء كان الزوج غائباً أو حاضراً. 
[ ٣ ] قوله : لعجزه ؛ هذا ليس بقيد احترازي بل ذكره لكونه مختلفا فيه ؛ ولأنه يعلم منه حكم ما إذا لم يعجز بالطريق الأولى، فإنّه إذا لم يجز التفريق لعجزه مع الاحتياج لم يجز عند القدرة عليه بالطريق الأولى، فإذا كان قادرا عليه وامتنع عن الإنفاق عليها يبيع الحاكم ماله عليه ويصرفه في نفقتها، فإن لم يجد ماله حبسه حتى ينفق عليها. كذا في «الفتح». 
(٤) قوله : وتؤمر ؛ أي من جانب القاضي، وفائدة ذلك : أن يتمكن صاحب الدين على أن يأخذ دينه من الزوج، فإنّ المرأة لو استدانت على الزوج بدون أمر القاضي ليس لرب الدين أن يرجع عليه، بل يطالبها بدينه ويأخذه منها، ثمّ هي ترجعُ على زوجها بما فرض لها القاضي، وهذا لأنَّ الاستدانة على الزوج إيجاب الدين عليه ، وليس لها ولاية عليه حتى يوجب ديناً عليه ، فيضم إلى ذلك أمر القاضي الذي له ولايةٌ عامة. كذا في «تحفة الفقهاء»، و«البناية». 
(٤) قوله : بالاستدانة... ؛ أي أن تنفق على نفسها بأن يأخذ من الغير بالقرض. أي تؤمر بأن تستقرض عليه، وتصرف إلى نفقتها حتى إِنْ غَنِيَ الرُّوجُ يؤدِّي فرضها، وهذا عندنا. وأما عند الشَّافِعِي له ، فالقاضي يفرق بينهما ؛ لأنَّه لا عَجَزَ عـن الإمساك بالمعروف ينوب القاضى منابهُ في التسريح بالإحسان. ويكون أداؤه على الزوج. 
[ ١ ] قوله : بأن تستقرض ؛ أي تأخذ على القرض على الزوج بأمر القاضي ، فيكون أداؤه عليه، وهذا صريحٌ في أنّ مرادهم بالاستدانة الاستقراض، وذكر الخصاف وغيره أنها الشراء بالنسيئة ؛ ليقضي الثمن من مال الزوج، ولا يخفى أنه لا يتيسر على المرأة في كل مرة، فقد لا تجد من يبيعُ منها بالنسيئة، فالأولى هو ما أشار إليه الشارح له. 
[ ٢ ] قوله : فرضها ؛ أي مقدار مفروضها ، وأشار به إلى أنّ أمر القاضي بالاستدانة عليه إنما يكون بعد فرضه وتقديره ، والحاصل : إنّه إذا عجز عن الإنفاق عليها واستغاثت المرأة بالقاضي يقدّر لها عليه مقداراً مناسباً، ثم يأمرها بالاستدانة عليه ، والتفصيل في «الذخيرة» و«الخلاصة»، وغيرهما من الفتاوى المعتبرة. 
[ ٣ ] قوله : وأما عند الشافعي ؛ حاصل مذهبه أنّ الزوج إذا أعسر عن النفقة فللقاضي أن يفرّق بينهما إذا طالبت الزوجة بذلك ، وكذا إذا غاب وتعذر تحصيل نفقتها منه على ما اختاره كثير من أصحاب مذهبه. والأصح المعتمد عندهم على ما ذكره ابن حَجَرٍ المكي وغيره أن لا فسخ ما دام موسراً، وإن انقطع خبره وتعذر استيفاء النفقة من ماله، نعم ؛ إن قامت البينة على إعساره وهو غائب يفرّق بينهما ؛ لثبوتِ العجز والقضاء على الغائب جائز عند الشافعي له. 
[ ٤ ] قوله : لأنه لما عجز ... الخ ؛ حاصله : أنّ الواجب على الزوج بنص الكتاب
It is not (obligatory) on a poor person according to the most sound opinion, and they are not separated due to his inability to provide it, and she is ordered to borrow on his behalf. (لا معسراً في الأصح) is to avoid the statement of Muhammad, for according to him, the maintenance of a servant is obligatory upon the poor husband. And (ولا يفرق بينهما لعجزه عنها، وتؤمر بالاستدانة عليه). His statement: 'لا معسراً' means the maintenance of a servant is not obligatory upon the husband if he is needy. Poverty and wealth here are estimated by the threshold of prohibition of charity, not by the threshold of obligation of zakat, and it is the growing wealth surplus to one's basic needs. The wealth that necessitates sadaqat al-fitr and udhiyah and makes it prohibited to take charity is owning the equivalent of two hundred dirhams surplus to one's needs. Thus in al-Binayah. His statement: 'ولا يفرّق' applies whether the husband is absent or present. His statement: 'لعجزه' is not a restrictive condition but was mentioned because it is disputed; and because from it one knows the ruling of when he is not unable by a priority, for if separation is not allowed due to his inability with need, it is not allowed when he is able by first priority. So if he is able but refuses to spend on her, the judge sells his property and spends it on her maintenance; if he finds no property, he imprisons him until he spends on her. Thus in al-Fath. His statement: 'وتؤمر' meaning by the judge; the benefit is that the creditor can take his debt from the husband. For if the woman borrows on the husband without the judge's order, the creditor cannot recourse to him, but demands the debt from her and takes it from her, then she recourses to her husband for what the judge imposed. This is because borrowing on the husband is imposing a debt on him, and she has no authority over him to impose a debt, so the judge's order, who has general authority, is added. Thus in Tuhfat al-Fuqaha' and al-Binayah. His statement: 'بالاستدانة' means she spends on herself by taking a loan from another. That is, she is ordered to borrow on his behalf and spend on her maintenance until, if the husband becomes wealthy, he pays what was imposed. This is our position. According to al-Shafi'i, the judge separates them because when he is unable to retain her with kindness, the judge takes his place in releasing with goodness. And its payment is upon the husband. His statement: 'بأن تستقرض' means she takes a loan on the husband by the judge's order, so its payment is upon him. This is explicit that by borrowing they mean taking a loan. Al-Khassaf and others mentioned that it is buying on credit to pay the price from the husband's wealth, but it is not easy for the woman every time; she may not find someone to sell to her on credit, so the preferable is what the commentator indicated. His statement: 'فرضها' meaning the amount imposed, and he indicated by this that the judge's order to borrow on him is only after imposing and estimating. The conclusion: when he is unable to spend on her and the woman seeks help from the judge, he estimates a suitable amount for her, then orders her to borrow on him. The details are in al-Dhakhirah, al-Khulasah, and other reliable fatwas. His statement: 'وأما عند الشافعي' – the essence of his school: if the husband is unable to pay maintenance, the judge can separate them if the wife demands it, and also if he is absent and it is difficult to obtain her maintenance from him, as chosen by many of his followers. But the most sound and relied-upon according to them, as mentioned by Ibn Hajar al-Makki and others, is that there is no annulment as long as he is wealthy, even if news of him is cut off and it is difficult to obtain maintenance from his wealth. Yes, if evidence of his inability is established while he is absent, the judge separates them due to the proof of inability, and judgment against the absent is permissible according to al-Shafi'i. His statement: 'لأنه لما عجز...' – its summary: that which is obligatory upon the husband by the text of the Book...
Source pages p. 544

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute and detailed commentary on the ruling of the wife's right to a servant (khādim) and the procedure when the husband is poor. The author begins by stating the relied-upon Hanafi position: a poor (mu‘sir) husband is not obligated to provide a servant for his wife. This contrasts with the view of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, who holds that even a poor husband must provide a servant. The term 'poor' here is defined by the threshold of being prohibited from receiving charity (i.e., owning 200 dirhams surplus to basic needs), not the zakat threshold. Next, the author explains that the couple are not to be separated due to the husband's inability to provide maintenance or a servant. This applies whether the husband is present or absent. The condition 'due to his inability' is not restrictive; rather, it is mentioned because it is a point of dispute. By implication, if separation is not allowed even when he is unable, it is certainly not allowed when he is able. In fact, if he is able but refuses to spend, the judge can sell his property or imprison him. The wife is then ordered by the judge to borrow (istidānah) for her maintenance on behalf of the husband. The benefit of the judge's order is that the creditor can then claim the debt from the husband. Without the order, the creditor would only have recourse against the wife. This is because imposing a debt on the husband requires authority, which only the judge has. The author clarifies that borrowing here means taking a loan (qarḍ), not buying on credit (nasī’ah), though some scholars like al-Khassaf allowed the latter. The judge first estimates the appropriate amount (farḍ) then orders the wife to borrow. Finally, the passage contrasts the Hanafi position with that of Imam al-Shafi‘i: in the Shafi‘i school, the judge can separate the couple if the husband is unable to pay maintenance and the wife demands it, based on the principle that the judge acts as the husband's deputy in releasing with kindness. However, the more sound Shafi‘i view (as per Ibn Hajar) is that separation only occurs if the husband is absent and his inability is proven. The Hanafi school maintains that separation is not allowed; the marriage continues and the debt remains on the husband.

Key Terms
معسر (mu‘sir): A husband who owns less than 200 dirhams surplus to basic needs, making him poor by the nisab of prohibition of charity.
نفقة الخادم (nafaqat al-khādim): The maintenance and expenses for a servant (maid or helper) for the wife, including food, clothing, and lodging.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on providing a servant (khādim) for the wife when the husband is poor (mu‘sir)?
A) It is obligatory on the husband regardless of his financial status.
B) It is not obligatory on a poor husband.
C) The wife must work to pay for the servant herself.
D) The judge must separate the couple if the husband cannot afford a servant.

Key Takeaway
A poor husband is not required to provide a servant; poverty is measured by owning less than 200 dirhams surplus (nisab of prohibition of charity).

Nafaqah — Maintenance
intermediate
