Borrowing and Non-Separation When Husband is Poor
Procedure of borrowing and ruling on non-separation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi procedure when a husband cannot pay maintenance: the wife borrows with the judge's order and the couple are not separated, and you will be able to compare this with the Shafi'i view on separation.

Core Idea
The couple are not separated due to poverty; the wife borrows with the judge's order, and the debt is on the husband.

When a husband is poor and cannot provide maintenance, the Hanafi school provides a remedy that preserves the marriage. The wife is ordered by the judge to borrow on the husband's behalf. This is contrasted with the Shafi'i school, which allows judicial separation. Understanding this difference is essential for knowing the rights and procedures in cases of spousal poverty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the situation
The husband is poor (mu‘sir) and unable to pay his wife's maintenance. The wife seeks relief from the judge.
2
State the Hanafi ruling: no separation
The couple are not separated due to the husband's poverty. This is the clear Hanafi position, regardless of whether the husband is present or absent.
The author says 'ولا يفرق بينهما لعجزه عنها' - they are not separated.
3
Explain the procedure: borrowing (istidānah)
The judge estimates a suitable maintenance amount (farḍ) and orders the wife to borrow on the husband's behalf. The benefit is that the creditor can claim the debt from the husband. Without the judge's order, the creditor would only have recourse against the wife.
The preferred method is taking a loan (qarḍ), not buying on credit (nasī’ah).
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i view
Imam al-Shafi‘i allows the judge to separate the couple if the husband is unable to pay. His basis: the husband cannot retain his wife with kindness (as per Quran), so the judge acts as his deputy to release her.
The Hanafi response is that inability to pay does not mean inability to retain with kindness; the wife can borrow and the debt remains.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In Hanafi, the marriage continues and the wife's maintenance becomes a debt. In Shafi‘i, the wife may obtain an annulment (faskh).

So if he is able but refuses to spend on her, the judge sells his property and spends it on her maintenance; if he finds no property, he imprisons him until he spends on her. Thus in al-Fath. His statement: 'وتؤمر' meaning by the judge; the benefit is that the creditor can take his debt from the husband. For if the woman borrows on the husband without the judge's order, the creditor cannot recourse to him, but demands the debt from her and takes it from her, then she recourses to her husband for what the judge imposed. This is because borrowing on the husband is imposing a debt on him, and she has no authority over him to impose a debt, so the judge's order, who has general authority, is added. Thus in Tuhfat al-Fuqaha' and al-Binayah. His statement: 'بالاستدانة' means she spends on herself by taking a loan from another. That is, she is ordered to borrow on his behalf and spend on her maintenance until, if the husband becomes wealthy, he pays what was imposed. This is our position. According to al-Shafi'i, the judge separates them because when he is unable to retain her with kindness, the judge takes his place in releasing with goodness. And its payment is upon the husband. His statement: 'بأن تستقرض' means she takes a loan on the husband by the judge's order, so its payment is upon him. This is explicit that by borrowing they mean taking a loan. Al-Khassaf and others mentioned that it is buying on credit to pay the price from the husband's wealth, but it is not easy for the woman every time; she may not find someone to sell to her on credit, so the preferable is what the commentator indicated. His statement: 'فرضها' meaning the amount imposed, and he indicated by this that the judge's order to borrow on him is only after imposing and estimating. The conclusion: when he is unable to spend on her and the woman seeks help from the judge, he estimates a suitable amount for her, then orders her to borrow on him. The details are in al-Dhakhirah, al-Khulasah, and other reliable fatwas. His statement: 'وأما عند الشافعي' – the essence of his school: if the husband is unable to pay maintenance, the judge can separate them if the wife demands it, and also if he is absent and it is difficult to obtain her maintenance from him, as chosen by many of his followers. But the most sound and relied-upon according to them, as mentioned by Ibn Hajar al-Makki and others, is that there is no annulment as long as he is wealthy, even if news of him is cut off and it is difficult to obtain maintenance from his wealth. Yes, if evidence of his inability is established while he is absent, the judge separates them due to the proof of inability, and judgment against the absent is permissible according to al-Shafi'i. His statement: 'لأنه لما عجز...' – its summary: that which is obligatory upon the husband by the text of the Book...
Source pages p. 544

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute and detailed commentary on the ruling of the wife's right to a servant (khādim) and the procedure when the husband is poor. The author begins by stating the relied-upon Hanafi position: a poor (mu‘sir) husband is not obligated to provide a servant for his wife. This contrasts with the view of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, who holds that even a poor husband must provide a servant. The term 'poor' here is defined by the threshold of being prohibited from receiving charity (i.e., owning 200 dirhams surplus to basic needs), not the zakat threshold. Next, the author explains that the couple are not to be separated due to the husband's inability to provide maintenance or a servant. This applies whether the husband is present or absent. The condition 'due to his inability' is not restrictive; rather, it is mentioned because it is a point of dispute. By implication, if separation is not allowed even when he is unable, it is certainly not allowed when he is able. In fact, if he is able but refuses to spend, the judge can sell his property or imprison him. The wife is then ordered by the judge to borrow (istidānah) for her maintenance on behalf of the husband. The benefit of the judge's order is that the creditor can then claim the debt from the husband. Without the order, the creditor would only have recourse against the wife. This is because imposing a debt on the husband requires authority, which only the judge has. The author clarifies that borrowing here means taking a loan (qarḍ), not buying on credit (nasī’ah), though some scholars like al-Khassaf allowed the latter. The judge first estimates the appropriate amount (farḍ) then orders the wife to borrow. Finally, the passage contrasts the Hanafi position with that of Imam al-Shafi‘i: in the Shafi‘i school, the judge can separate the couple if the husband is unable to pay maintenance and the wife demands it, based on the principle that the judge acts as the husband's deputy in releasing with kindness. However, the more sound Shafi‘i view (as per Ibn Hajar) is that separation only occurs if the husband is absent and his inability is proven. The Hanafi school maintains that separation is not allowed; the marriage continues and the debt remains on the husband.

Key Terms
استدانة (istidānah): The act of borrowing money, specifically by the wife on behalf of the husband under the judge's order, to cover her maintenance expenses.
معسر (mu‘sir): A poor husband who owns less than 200 dirhams surplus to basic needs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens when a husband is poor and cannot pay maintenance?
A) The judge separates the couple.
B) The judge orders the wife to borrow on the husband's behalf.
C) The wife must work to support herself.
D) The husband is imprisoned until he pays.

Key Takeaway
The couple are not separated due to poverty; the wife borrows with the judge's order, and the debt is on the husband.

Nafaqah — Maintenance

Hanafi vs Shafi'i on Judicial Separation for Non-Payment of Maintenance
Hanafi commentary contrasting their stance on judicial separation with the Shafi'i view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on judicial separation for non-payment of maintenance and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the madhhab dispute map.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school does not separate a couple for non-payment; the wife borrows, but if borrowing fails, a Shafi'i deputy may separate by istihsān.

This lesson examines a key difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on whether a judge can dissolve a marriage when a husband cannot pay maintenance. The Hanafi position, based on the Qur'an and analogy, maintains that the wife should borrow and the marriage remains intact. However, the Shafi'i school relies on a hadith to permit separation. We will analyze the proofs and the practical consequences of this ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What is the ruling according to the Hanafi school? What does the judge order the wife to do?
The judge does not separate; he orders the wife to borrow on the husband's account.
2
Identify the Shafi'i view
What does Imam al-Shafi'i hold? On what basis?
The judge may separate if the husband is insolvent and the wife demands it.
3
Follow the Shafi'i proof
What hadith do they cite? How do they derive the ruling?
Hadith of Abu Hurairah: 'Separate between them.' They consider it a clear command.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school rebut the hadith? What alternative proofs do they offer?
The hadith is weak (ma'lool); it is only a mawqūf statement of Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib. They also argue by qiyas that maintenance is subsidiary.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference? When would a Hanafi judge resort to istihsān?
In a Hanafi court, the wife cannot get a divorce for poverty; she must borrow. Only if borrowing is impossible may a Shafi'i deputy be appointed to separate.

والسنة أحد الأمرين :
إما أن يمسكها بمعروف.
وإما أن يسرحها ويطلق عنانها.
وقد عجز بعجزه عن النفقة لإعساره عن الإمساكِ بالمعروف، فيجب عليه أن يسرحها ويفارقها ، وإذ لم يفعله وتضررت المرأةُ قامَ القاضي مقامه ؛ لكونه ذا ولاية عامة، فيفرّق بينهما ، ونظيره التفريق في الزوج العنين والمجبوب.

The Book of Divorce: Chapter of Maintenance. Meaning, she is ordered to borrow on his behalf, and it is spent on her maintenance, so that if the husband becomes wealthy, he will pay her obligatory amount. This is with us. As for al-Shafi'i—may Allah have mercy on him—the judge separates between them, because he is unable to retain her with kindness, so the judge takes his place in releasing her with kindness. And its payment is upon the husband. [1] His statement: 'to borrow' means she takes a loan on the husband by order of the judge, so the payment is upon him. This is clear that what they mean by 'istidānah' is borrowing. Al-Khashshāf and others mentioned that it is purchase on credit so that the price is paid from the husband's wealth. It is not hidden that this is not easy for the woman every time, as she may not find someone to sell to her on credit. So the first is what the commentator indicated. The summary: when he is unable to spend on her and the woman appeals to the judge, he assesses an appropriate amount for her, then orders her to borrow it. The details are in 'al-Dhakhīrah' and 'al-Khulāṣah' and other reliable fatwa collections. Similarly, if he is absent and obtaining her maintenance from him is difficult, according to what many of his companions chose. Yes, if proof of his insolvency is established while he is absent, the judge separates between them due to proven inability, and judgment against the absent is permissible according to al-Shafi'i. [4] His statement: 'because when he became unable...' etc. Its summary: what is obligatory upon the husband according to the text of the Book. And our companions—may Allah have mercy on them—when they observed necessity in separation, because repelling a continuous need is not easy by borrowing, and it is apparent that she will not find someone to lend to her, and the husband's wealth is a hypothetical matter, they deemed it good that the judge appoint a deputy of the Shafi'i madhhab who separates between them. And the Sunnah is one of two matters: either to retain her with kindness, or to release her and set her free. And he is unable due to his inability to spend, being insolvent for retention with kindness, so it becomes obligatory upon him to release and separate from her. And if he does not do so and the woman is harmed, the judge takes his place because he has general authority, so he separates between them. Its analogy is the separation in the case of the impotent (al-'innīn) and the castrated (al-majbūb). And we say: separation in the case of castration and impotence is only permitted due to the loss of the main purpose of marriage, which is procreation and reproduction. But wealth is not like that, for it is subsidiary in marriage, so its loss does not necessitate separation. And her continuous need can be repelled by ordering borrowing on his behalf, so the woman is not harmed in this situation like her harm in castration and impotence, such that release with kindness becomes necessary and the judge takes his place. And al-Shafi'i may argue with the hadith of Abu Hurairah that the Prophet ﷺ said regarding a man who does not find what to spend on his wife: 'Separate between them.' It is reported by al-Daraqutni and al-Bayhaqi. This is a clear text on the matter, so no opposing statement is accepted, for the statement is what the Messenger said. Otherwise, the statement of anyone else opposing his statement is not accepted, whoever it may be. However, this hadith is defective (ma'lool), not established, as verified by al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar in 'Talkhīṣ al-Ḥabīr'. He scrutinized that al-Daraqutni was mistaken in its narration, and al-Bayhaqi followed him in his error. Yes, a similar hadith is narrated from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib as a mawqūf report. In summary, if a marfū' hadith with a reliable chain that rules for separation is established in this chapter, we accept it wholeheartedly. Otherwise, the statement is what our companions said, unless need calls for separation, i.e., when borrowing is also not possible. [1] His statement: 'deemed it good' is the result of his statement: 'when they observed...' Abu Hafs al-Isturūshnī said, as a Shafi'i, in his 'Fuṣūl': When inability is proven by the testimony of witnesses, if the judge separates between them, his judgment is executed. And if he is a Hanafi, he should not judge contrary to his madhhab, unless...
Source pages p. 545

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary (sharh) on a ruling from the chapter on maintenance (nafaqah) in the context of divorce. The base ruling states that when a husband cannot pay maintenance, the wife is ordered to borrow on his behalf, and if the husband later becomes wealthy, he must repay. The commentator opens by clarifying the meaning of 'istidānah': it is borrowing (qard) rather than deferred purchase, though some scholars like al-Khashshāf mention the latter. The preferable view is borrowing because a wife may not find a seller willing to sell on credit. Then the commentator explains that the judge must first assess the appropriate amount of maintenance before ordering the wife to borrow. Next, the passage contrasts the Hanafi position with that of Imam al-Shafi'i. In the Shafi'i school, if the husband is insolvent, the judge can separate the couple if the wife demands it, and similarly if the husband is absent and his wealth is inaccessible. However, the more correct Shafi'i view (as per Ibn Hajar al-Makkī) is that no rescission occurs as long as the husband is capable, even if his whereabouts are unknown—only if his inability is proven while absent does separation occur. The Hanafi commentators then address a potential objection: if the wife cannot find anyone to lend her money, then she is in dire need. In such a case, some Hanafi scholars have deemed it permissible (istihsān) for the judge to appoint a Shafi'i deputy to separate them, because the continuous need cannot be met otherwise. The commentator then lays out the Hanafi proof: The Qur'an obligates either retaining the wife with kindness or releasing her with kindness. Since the husband is unable to do either due to poverty, he must release her. If he does not, the judge, having general authority, can separate them. This is analogous to the case of an impotent or castrated husband, where the judge separates because the main purpose of marriage—procreation—is lost. However, the Hanafi response is that the analogy is flawed: money is subsidiary, not the primary purpose. The wife's need can be met by borrowing, and if borrowing is possible, she is not harmed like the wife of an impotent man. Therefore, the judge does not separate. The Shafi'i rely on a hadith from Abu Hurairah (marfū'an) that the Prophet ordered separation. The commentator, citing Ibn Hajar, points out that this hadith is defective (ma'lool); al-Daraqutni was mistaken in raising it to the Prophet, and al-Bayhaqi followed his error. It is only authentic as a statement of Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib (mawqūf). Thus, the hadith is not proof for separation. In conclusion, if a sound marfū' hadith is established, it is accepted; otherwise, the Hanafi view stands unless borrowing is also impossible, in which case the need may justify separation as per the istihsān mentioned.

Key Terms
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance: the financial support a husband must provide for his wife covering food, clothing, shelter, etc.
استقراض (istiqrāḍ): Borrowing: the wife takes a loan from someone on behalf of the husband by order of the judge, making the debt the husband's responsibility.
جب (jabb): Castration: the removal of the male reproductive organs, making procreation impossible.
عنة ('innah): Impotence: the inability to achieve sexual intercourse.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference: a method of reasoning that departs from strict analogy (qiyas) in favor of a more convenient or equitable ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) She may immediately seek a divorce from the judge.
B) She is ordered to borrow money on the husband's account.
C) The husband is imprisoned until he pays.
D) The wife must simply be patient and cannot take any action.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's refusal to separate for non-payment is based on the subsidiary nature of maintenance and the hadith's weakness; the wife's remedy is borrowing, not divorce.

Nafaqah — Maintenance

Past Maintenance and Changing Financial Status in Hanafi Nafaqah
Ruling that past maintenance lapses unless fixed by qadi, and wife can claim increased maintenance if husband becomes wealthy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on past maintenance and apply it to scenarios where the husband's financial condition changes.

Core Idea
Past maintenance is waived unless decreed by a qadi; maintenance rates adjust to the husband's current ability.

This lesson addresses two common questions in Hanafi family law: Is a husband liable for maintenance he failed to pay in the past? And if his financial condition improves, can the wife demand a higher amount retroactively? The answer turns on the nature of maintenance as a periodic support, not a debt, and the role of the qadi's decree in fixing liability. We will explore the reasoning from al-Hidayah and its commentaries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage gives two separate rulings: (1) past maintenance is waived unless fixed by a qadi or agreement; (2) if the husband goes from poor to wealthy, the wife can claim the higher amount from the time she sues.
2
Understand the rationale for past maintenance lapse
Maintenance is not a compensation for the wife's sexual benefits (which are covered by mahr) but a support (silah) arising from her confinement. Therefore, it does not become a fixed debt until a qadi decrees it or the couple agrees.
3
Note the exception to the lapse
If a qadi had previously fixed an amount or the couple agreed on a specific sum, that amount becomes a debt and does not lapse. Additionally, if the wife borrowed with the qadi's permission, that debt remains after separation.
4
Recognize the intra-madhhab nuance
The general rule is that all past maintenance lapses, but al-Dhakhirah reports an exception: maintenance of less than a month does not lapse. This nuance is considered in fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَن فُرِضَتْ لعساره فأيسر، تممّ نفقة يسارِهِ إن طلبت ، وتسقط نفقة مدةٍ مضت إلا إذا سبق فرض قاض
[1] قوله : ومن فرضت...الخ؛ حاصل هذه المسألة : أنه إذا كان الزوج معسراً ففرض لها القاضي بنفقة الإعسار ثم صار موسراً فخاصمته يتمم لها نفقة اليسار من حين الخصومة ؛ لأنَّ النفقة تختلفُ يساراً أو عساراً، وما قضى به تقدير لنفقة لم تجب ؛ لأنها تجب شيئاً فشيئاً، فإذا تبدل حاله فلها المطالبة بتمام حقها، وكذا الحكم إذا كانت معسرة فأيسرت.
[٢] قوله : وتسقط.... الخ ؛ الأصل في هذا الباب أنّ النفقة إنما تجب على الزوج لاحتباس الزوجة ، ومع ذلك فهو ليس بعوض لشيء، فإنّ منافع البضع عوضها المهر، بل هي صلة، فلا يستحكم الوجوب فيها إلا بالقضاء أو باصطلاحها على شيء، فإنّه بمنزلة القضاء ؛ لأنّ ولايته على نفسه أقوى من ولاية القاضي. فإذا لم ينفق عليها غائباً أو حاضراً سقطت نفقة مدة مضت إلا أن يكون تعلّق بها تقدير قاض أو تراضيهما على شيء معين، فيجب ذلك المفروض، ويسقط هو أيضاً بالفرقة قبل القبض بطلاقه أو موته إلا إذا استدانت عليه بأمر قاض فلا يسقط هو بعد الفرقة. كذا في «الهداية» وشروحها.
[٣] قوله : مدة ؛ ظاهره إطلاق الزمان ولو قليلاً ، لكن في «الذخيرة»: إنّ نفقة ما دون الشهر لا تسقط.
Whoever was assigned maintenance for his poverty and then becomes wealthy, he completes the maintenance of wealth if she demands it. The maintenance of a past period lapses unless preceded by a qadi's decree. [1] The sum of this issue: if the husband is poor and the qadi decrees maintenance of poverty for her, then he becomes wealthy and she sues him, he completes the maintenance of wealth from the time of suit, because maintenance differs according to wealth or poverty, and what was decreed is a determination for maintenance that was not yet due, because it becomes due bit by bit. So if his status changes, she has the right to demand full entitlement. Likewise, if she was poor and then became wealthy. [2] The principle in this chapter is that maintenance is only obligatory on the husband because of the wife's confinement to him, and yet it is not a compensation for anything, for the benefits of the vulva are compensated by the dowry; rather, it is a support. So obligation does not become firmly established in it except by a qadi's decree or by her agreement to something, for that has the force of a qadi's decree because his authority over himself is stronger than the qadi's authority. If he does not spend on her, whether absent or present, the maintenance of a past period lapses, unless a qadi's determination is attached to it or they mutually agree on a specific thing, then that fixed amount becomes due, and even that lapses by separation before receipt through his divorce or death, except if she borrowed by order of a qadi, then it does not lapse after separation. As in al-Hidayah and its commentaries. [3] The word 'period' apparently applies to time even if little, but in al-Dhakhirah: maintenance of less than a month does not lapse.
Source pages p. 547

Commentary
This passage expounds two rulings: first, if maintenance was decreed for an indigent (mu'sir) husband and he later becomes wealthy (muyassar), the wife can claim the higher amount from the time she files suit. This is because maintenance varies with financial status and becomes due incrementally. Second, past maintenance is generally waived (sāqit) unless a qadi had previously fixed an amount or the couple had agreed on a specific sum. The rationale is that maintenance is not a debt or exchange for benefits—the wife's sexual benefits are compensated by the mahr—but a support (silah) arising from the wife's confinement. Therefore, its obligation is only firmly established by a judicial decree or mutual agreement. If no decree or agreement exists, the husband is not liable for past non-payment, even if he was absent. However, if the wife borrowed with the qadi's permission to cover her needs, that debt remains. There is a nuance in al-Dhakhirah that maintenance of less than a month does not lapse.

Key Terms
الفرض (al-fard): The qadi's judicial decree fixing an amount of maintenance.
الإعسار (al-i'sar): The state of being unable to pay maintenance due to poverty.
الإيسار (al-aisar): The state of being wealthy, allowing for higher maintenance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under Hanafi law, what is the effect of a qadi's decree on past maintenance?
A) It makes the entire past maintenance from marriage date binding.
B) It only fixes the amount for the period after the decree; past maintenance before the decree still lapses.
C) It makes the maintenance due from the date of the decree onward, and also the period before if the decree specifies it.
D) It has no effect on past maintenance; only the couple's agreement can make past maintenance binding.

Key Takeaway
Past maintenance is not automatically owed; it must be fixed by a qadi or agreement to become a debt.

Maintenance Adjustment Upon Husband's Improved Financial Status
Ruling on increasing maintenance when husband's financial condition improves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on increasing maintenance when the husband becomes wealthy and explain the reason (ta'lil) that the increase applies from the time of litigation.

Core Idea
Maintenance varies with ability; a judge's prior estimation does not fix it forever.

When a husband's financial condition changes after a judge has already set maintenance based on his previous state, a new claim is possible. This ruling shows how nafaqah is a flexible ongoing obligation that adjusts to the husband's ability, with the increase taking effect from the date the wife files a lawsuit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The husband was poor (mu'sir) and the judge fixed maintenance based on that poverty. Then the husband becomes wealthy (mūsir).
The key is the change in financial status after the judge's decree.
2
State the ruling
The wife can demand the increased maintenance corresponding to the husband's new wealth. The increase is effective from the time she files the lawsuit (khugumah), not from the moment the husband became wealthy.
The text says 'yutammimu lahā nafaqat al-yasār min ḥīni al-khuṣūmah'.
3
Explain the reason (ta'lil)
Maintenance (nafaqah) varies based on the husband's ability (yasar/usur). The judge's earlier decree was merely an estimation for a future obligation that had not yet become due because maintenance becomes obligatory bit by bit (shay'an fa shay'an). When the condition changes, the obligation adjusts.
The commentary states: 'li'anna al-nafaqata takhtalifu yasāran aw 'usāran, wa mā qaḍā bihi taqdīrun li nafaqatin lam tajib; li'annahā tajibu shay'an fa shay'an'.
4
Apply the analogous case
The same principle applies if the wife's financial status changes (from poor to wealthy). The text says 'ka dhālika al-ḥukm idhā kānat mu'siratan fa aysarat' – the maintenance adjusts, likely allowing the husband to reduce it? Actually careful: in Hanafi, maintenance is based on husband's ability, not wife's, but the 'same ruling' likely means the adjustment takes effect from the time of litigation, not that the amount changes based on wife's wealth. We'll note this nuance.
Some later Hanafi interpretations do allow reduction if wife becomes wealthy, but the text's primary focus is on husband's ability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَن فُرِضَتْ لعساره فأيسر، تم نفقة يسارِهِ إن طلبت. [1] قوله : ومن فرضت...الخ؛ حاصل هذه المسألة : أنه إذا كان الزوج معسراً ففرض لها القاضي بنفقة الإعسار ثم صار موسراً فخاصمته يتمم لها نفقة اليسار من حين الخصومة ؛ لأنَّ النفقة تختلفُ يساراً أو عساراً، وما قضى به تقدير لنفقة لم تجب ؛ لأنها تجب شيئاً فشيئاً، فإذا تبدل حاله فلها المطالبة بتمام حقها، وكذا الحكم إذا كانت معسرة فأيسرت.
And whoever (i.e., the wife) had maintenance fixed for her based on his (her husband's) hardship (i.e., his being poor), then he becomes wealthy, she completes (receives) the maintenance of his wealth if she demands it. [Commentary] The summary of this issue: If the husband is poor and the judge fixed for her maintenance of the poor, then he becomes wealthy and she litigates, he completes for her the maintenance of the wealthy from the time of litigation; because maintenance differs based on wealth or poverty, and what the judge decreed is an estimation for maintenance that had not become obligatory, because it becomes obligatory bit by bit. So when his condition changes, she has the right to demand the completion of her right. Similarly, the ruling if she was poor and then becomes wealthy.
Source pages p. 547

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a ruling from the base text of al-Wiqayah. The core issue: when a husband's financial status changes after a judge has already fixed maintenance based on his previous condition. The ruling states that the wife can demand the increased maintenance corresponding to his new wealth from the time she files a lawsuit (khugumah). The reasoning (ta'lil) is that maintenance (nafaqah) varies according to the husband's ability (yasar/aysar vs. i'sar). The judge's previous decree was merely a quantification for a future obligation that had not yet fallen due, because maintenance becomes owed moment by moment. Therefore, when the underlying condition changes, the wife is entitled to claim the full amount appropriate to the new condition. This applies equally if the wife goes from poverty to wealth (i.e., the husband's obligation may decrease).

Key Terms
نفقة (nafaqah): Financial maintenance owed by husband to wife for food, clothing, and lodging
عسر (usur): Financial hardship or poverty of the husband
يسر (yasar): Financial ease or wealth
خصومة (khugumah): Lawsuit or litigation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) She gets increased maintenance from the time the judge gave the first ruling.
B) She gets increased maintenance from the time she files the lawsuit.
C) She does not get increased maintenance because the previous ruling is final.
D) She gets increased maintenance only if the husband consents.

Key Takeaway
The judge's initial maintenance order is provisional and can be adjusted when the husband's financial ability changes; the adjustment takes effect from the date the wife files a lawsuit.

Dropping of Past Maintenance: The Principle and Its Exceptions
Principle-based ruling with talil (reasoning) and cross-madhhab comparison

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that past maintenance not fixed by judge or agreement drops, and apply the exceptions to determine when fixed maintenance becomes binding and when it drops.

Core Idea
Past maintenance is a gift, not a debt — it drops unless fixed by judge or agreement, and even then drops at death/divorce before receipt.

This passage from al-Hidayah lays down a foundational principle: maintenance owed for past periods is not automatically enforceable. Because maintenance is classified as a gift (silah) rather than a quid pro quo (like dower), it does not become a binding debt unless a judge or the spouses themselves fix an amount. Even after fixation, the obligation lapses if death or divorce occurs before the wife actually receives the payment, with a narrow exception for borrowing with judicial permission. This lesson walks you through the rule, the underlying rationale, and the cross-madhhab dispute with al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core rule
The passage begins by stating that past maintenance drops. Understand the default: if the husband did not spend for a past period, that maintenance is not owed.
This is the foundation; exceptions follow.
2
Recognize the exceptions
Two exceptions: (1) a judge's decree fixing the amount, or (2) mutual agreement between spouses on a specific sum. Why does mutual agreement count? Because the wife's authority over herself is stronger than the judge's.
Taradi (mutual agreement) is treated like a judicial decree.
3
Understand the legal nature (silah)
Maintenance is characterized as a gift (silah), not compensation ('iwad). The benefits of the vulva are compensated by the mahr. This silah nature explains why past maintenance drops: a gift is not a binding debt until possession is taken.
This is the key rationale distinguishing Hanafi from Shafi'i.
4
Trace the post-fixation rule
Even after fixation by judge or agreement, the amount drops if separation (death or divorce) occurs before the wife receives it. Only borrowing with the judge's permission preserves the obligation.
Borrowing with judicial permission transforms the silah into a debt that survives.
5
Compare with Shafi'i position
The text notes that al-Shafi'i holds fixed maintenance becomes a debt that does not drop at death. Identify the thamarah al-khilaf: in practice, Hanafi relieves the husband if wife dies before receipt; Shafi'i obliges payment to her heirs.
This ikhtilaf stems from different conceptions of maintenance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتسقط نفقة مدةٍ مضت إلا إذا سبق فرض قاض. [2] قوله : وتسقط.... الخ؛ الأصل في هذا الباب أنّ النفقة إنما تجب على الزوج لاحتباس الزوجة ، ومع ذلك فهو ليس بعوض لشيء، فإنّ منافع البضع عوضها المهر، بل هي صلة، فلا يستحكم الوجوب فيها إلا بالقضاء أو باصطلاحها على شيء، فإنّه بمنزلة القضاء ؛ لأنّ ولايته على نفسه أقوى من ولاية القاضي. فإذا لم ينفق عليها غائباً أو حاضراً سقطت نفقة مدة مضت إلا أن يكون تعلّق بها تقدير قاض أو تراضيهما على شيء معين، فيجب ذلك المفروض، ويسقط هو أيضاً بالفرقة قبل القبض بطلاقه أو موته إلا إذا استدانت عليه بأمر قاض فلا يسقط هو بعد الفرقة. كذا في «الهداية» وشروحها. [3] قوله : مدة ; ظاهره إطلاق الزمان ولو قليلاً، لكن في «الذخيرة»: إنّ نفقة ما دون الشهر لا تسقط.
أو رضيا بشيء، فتجب لما مَضَى ما داما حيين فإن مات أحدهما أو طلقها قبل قبض سَقَط المفروض إلا إذا استدانت بأمر قاض)(۱) : هذا عندنا ، وأما عند الشَّافِعِي ) ه ، فلا تسقط بالموت ، بل تصير دينا عليه. [1] أي يسقط المفروض بموت أحدهما وبتطليقها ؛ لأنه صلة والصلة تسقط بالموت، وهذا لم يأمرها القاضي بالاستدانة على الزوج، فاستدانت ثم مات أحدهما لا يبطل بذلك. ينظر : شرح ابن ملك (ق ۱۲۰ /أ). [2] ينظر: ((مغني المحتاج»(٣: ٤٤١)، وغيره.
And maintenance for a period that has passed drops [i.e., is not owed] unless a judge's decree preceded. [Commentary 2] The principle in this chapter is that maintenance is only obligatory on the husband due to the wife's confinement [to the marriage], and despite that it is not a compensation for anything; for the benefits of the vulva are compensated by the mahr (dower). Rather, it is a gift (silah). Thus, obligation is not firmly established except by a judge's ruling or by her agreeing on something, for that is in the position of a judge's ruling because her authority over herself is stronger than the authority of the judge. So if he did not spend on her while he was absent or present, maintenance for a past period drops unless a judge's estimation or their mutual agreement on a specific amount is attached to it. Then that fixed amount becomes obligatory, and it also drops by separation (divorce or death) before receipt unless she borrowed with the judge's permission, then it does not drop after separation. Thus in al-Hidayah and its commentaries. [Commentary 3] His statement: 'a period' – its apparent meaning is the absolute time even if little, but in al-Dhakhirah: maintenance for less than a month does not drop.
Or they agree on something, then it becomes obligatory for the past as long as both are alive; if one dies or he divorces her before receipt, the fixed amount drops except if she borrowed with the judge's permission. ... This is according to us (Hanafis). As for al-Shafi'i (May Allah have mercy on him), it does not drop by death but becomes a debt upon him. [Footnote 1] That is, the fixed amount drops by death of one or by her divorce; because it is a gift and the gift drops by death. And this is if the judge did not order her to borrow upon the husband; if she borrowed then one dies, it does not become void. Look at Sharh Ibn Malik (folio 120a). [Footnote 2] Look at Mughni al-Muhtaj (3:441) and others.
Source pages p. 547

Commentary
This passage explains the foundational principle regarding maintenance for past periods. The commentary from al-Hidayah states the rule: maintenance owed for past time (when the husband was not spending) drops and is not enforceable unless there was a prior judicial decree or a mutual agreement between spouses fixing the amount. This is because maintenance (nafaqah) under Hanafi law is characterized as a gift (silah) and not a price for the wife's sexual availability (that is the dower). As a gift, it does not become a binding debt until it is actually determined by a judge or by the spouses' own agreement. Once such a determination is made, that fixed amount becomes obligatory. However, if the husband divorces or one of them dies before the wife actually receives the fixed sum, it drops (i.e., is no longer owed), unless the wife had borrowed money with the judge's permission to cover her needs. The text also notes an exception for very short periods: according to al-Dhakhirah, maintenance for less than a month does not drop. The passage then clarifies the Hanafi position against the Shafi'i view: al-Shafi'i holds that the fixed maintenance does not drop upon death but becomes a debt that must be paid from the estate. The Hanafi position is based on the nature of maintenance as a gift that is not intended to survive death.

Key Terms
صلة (silah): A gift or gratuity, as opposed to a compensatory payment (i.e., maintenance is a form of support, not a price for sexual enjoyment)
قضاء (qada): A judge's decree or ruling fixing the amount of maintenance
تراض (taradi): Mutual agreement between spouses on a specific amount of maintenance

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband did not pay maintenance for two months, and the wife never went to court. Later they divorce. According to Hanafi law, what is the status of that past maintenance?
A) It must be paid as a debt.
B) It drops and is not owed because it was not fixed by a judge or agreement.
C) It is owed only if the wife can prove she asked for it.
D) The husband must pay it to the wife's heirs if she dies.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, past maintenance is a gift (silah) that drops unless fixed by judge or agreement, and even then drops at death or divorce before receipt unless the wife borrowed with judicial permission.

Advance Maintenance Not Refundable Upon Death or Divorce
Ruling that prepaid maintenance is non-refundable upon death or divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on prepaid maintenance and explain why it is not refundable upon death or divorce before the period ends.

Core Idea
Once advanced maintenance is paid, it is a completed gift — non-refundable even if the marriage ends.

This lesson examines a specific Hanafi ruling regarding prepaid maintenance (nafaqah mu'ajjalah). When a spouse pays maintenance in advance for a future period, and the marriage ends due to death or divorce before that period expires, the payer cannot reclaim the unused portion. The passage establishes this rule on the authority of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and extends it to payments made by third parties like the husband's father.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage discusses prepaid maintenance for a fixed period (e.g., six months) when one spouse dies or divorce occurs before the period ends.
2
State the ruling
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, no portion of the prepaid maintenance is refundable. The text says 'لا يُسْتَرَدُّ منها شيء' (nothing is refunded).
3
Understand the reasoning
The payment is considered a completed gift (silah) that has been delivered. Once delivered, it cannot be reclaimed, similar to a gift.
4
Apply to analogous case
The text cites al-Walwaljiyyah: if the husband's father pays maintenance for his son's wife, then the husband divorces her, the father cannot reclaim the amount. This is by analogy to the husband himself paying.
The fatwa is upon Abu Yusuf's view.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that advance maintenance is non-refundable, based on Abu Yusuf's opinion as affirmed in al-Walwaljiyyah.
Imam Muhammad may have differed, but his view is not adopted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تُسْتَرَدُّ معجلةُ مُدَّةٍ مات أحدهما قبلها : أي إذا عُجِّلت نفقةُ مُدَّة ، كستَّةِ أشهر مثلاً، فمات أحدهما قبلها ، كما إذا مات عند مُضي شهرٍ لا يُسْتَرَدُّ منها شيء عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف. قال في «الولوالجية»: أبو الزوج إذا دفع نفقة امرأة ابنه مئة ثمَّ طلّقها الزوج ليس للأب أن يسترد ما دفع ؛ لأنه لو أعطاها الزوج ، والمسألة بحالها لم يكن له ذلك عند أبي يوسف له ، وعليه الفتوى ، فكذا إذا أعطاها أبوه. انتهى.
And the prepaid maintenance for a period if one of them dies before it [ends] is not refunded. That is, if maintenance is advanced for a period, for example six months, and one of them dies before it [ends], like if he dies after the passing of one month, nothing is refunded from it according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. He said in al-Walwaljiyyah: The father of the husband, if he pays one hundred as maintenance for his son's wife, then the husband divorces her, the father may not reclaim what he paid; because if the husband had given it, and the case is the same, he would not have it (the right to reclaim) according to Abu Yusuf, and upon it is the fatwa. So likewise if his father gave it. End.
Source pages p. 548

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario where maintenance for a future period (e.g., six months) is paid in advance (mu'ajjalah). If one of the spouses dies before the end of that period, the prepaid amount is not refundable. This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf. The reasoning is that once the payment is made, it is a completed gift (silah) and does not become subject to refund. The text then cites al-Walwaljiyyah extending this ruling to a case where the husband's father pays the maintenance: if the husband divorces the wife, the father cannot recover the amount paid, because if the husband himself had paid and then divorced, he would also not be able to reclaim it according to Abu Yusuf, and that is the relied-upon fatwa. This reinforces the principle that advance maintenance is a one-way transfer that is not contingent on the continuation of the marriage.

Key Terms
معجلة (mu'ajjalah): Prepaid or advanced payment, here referring to maintenance paid upfront for a future period
استرداد (istirdad): Legal recovery or reclaiming of property already given

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It must be refunded to the husband.
B) It becomes part of the wife's estate.
C) It is not refunded; the husband cannot reclaim it.
D) It is given to the wife's heirs.

Key Takeaway
Prepaid maintenance is a completed gift and cannot be reclaimed once paid.

Nafaqah — Maintenance

Advanced Maintenance Payment: Reclaimability Upon Death – Intra-Hanafi and Cross-Madhhab Analysis
Explains whether advanced maintenance can be reclaimed upon death of a spouse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on reclaiming advanced maintenance (mu'ajjala) upon death, distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
An advance on maintenance is a gift once possessed; death does not reverse it – Hanafi majority view.

In Hanafi law, maintenance (nafaqah) is an obligation on the husband. But what happens if he pays for a future period in advance and then dies? The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains an intra-Hanafi disagreement: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say no reclaim; Muhammad says the excess beyond a month is reclaimed. The commentator also contrasts the Shafi'i view, which treats maintenance as compensation for being withheld (ihtibas). This lesson dissects the reasoning and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and divergent views
Recognize that the act is payment of maintenance in advance for a fixed period. Note the three positions: Abu Hanifa & Abu Yusuf (no reclaim), Muhammad (reclaim excess over one month), and al-Shafi'i (reclaim unexpired portion).
Machine-draft: The passage clearly states each view.
2
Analyze the nature of maintenance: gift or compensation?
Understand the core difference: Hanafi majority says maintenance is a gift (sila) that becomes owned upon possession, while Shafi'i says it is compensation (iwad) for being withheld (ihtibas). This determines reclaimability.
The commentary explains the reasoning: 'li-annahu silatum ittasala biha al-qabd'.
3
Trace the analogy with a gift (hiba)
See how the Hanafi school analogizes maintenance to a gift: once possession (qabd) occurs, ownership is complete, and death of either party does not revoke it. This is the key qiyas.
The text says 'kama fi al-hibah'.
4
Examine Imam Muhammad's qualification
Muhammad distinguishes by amount: if the advance is for a month or less, it is treated as current and not reclaimed; if more, the excess is reclaimed. This suggests a different view on the nature of the payment.
This is a nawadir narration via Ibn Rustom.
5
Evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument and Hanafi response
The Shafi'i ruling treats the advance as compensation; the Hanafi response is that maintenance is not like other compensations, as evidenced by the consensus that if the goods perish without consumption, nothing is reclaimed.
The passage concludes with the Hanafi rebuttal: 'law halakat nafaqah mu'ajjalatun min ghayri istihlak... la yustaraddu shay'un bi al-ijma''.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ولا تُسْتَرَدُّ معجلةُ مُدَّةٍ مات أحدهما قبلها : أي إذا عُجِّلت نفقةُ مُدَّة، كستَّةِ أشهر مثلاً، فمات أحدهما قبلها، كما إذا مات عند مُضي شهرٍ لا يُسْتَرَدُّ منها شيء عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف محمد لأنها صلة اتصل بها القبض، فبالموتُ سقط الرجوع كما في الهبة، وعند الشَّافِعِي تحتسب نفقةُ ما مَضَى، وهو شهر للزوجة، ونفقة خمسة أشهر تسترد؛ لأنها عوض عما تستحق عليه بالاحتباس. وبه علم أن عدم الاسترداد يشترك فيه الموت والطلاق ولم يذكره المصنف له؛ لعدم ذكره كره في «الهداية». [١] قوله : لأنها صلة...الخ ؛ حاصله : أنّ النفقة صلةٌ في نفسها وإن وجبت جزاءً للاحتباس، والصلاتُ تملك بعد القبض، ويسقط حق الرجوع فيها عند الموت. [٢] قوله : كما في الهبة ؛ فإنّه إذا ملك الموهوب له الموهوب بالقبض، ثم مات الواهب أو الموهوب له لا يجوز رجوع الواهب أو ورثته فيها. [٣] قوله : وعند محمد؛ هذا رواية ابن رستم عنه، وفي رواية عنه : إنها إذا قبضت نفقة شهر فما دونها لا تسترد منها شيء؛ لأنه يسير، فصار في حكم الحال وما زاد علیه تسترد. [٤] قوله : لأنها عوض.... الخ ؛ حاصله : إنّ النفقة إنمـا تجـب لـهـا عـلـيـه بـسبب الاحتباس فهي عوض عنه، فإذا مات أحدهما قبل مضي تلك المدة التي عجلت ليها نفقتها بطل الاستحقاق لفوات الاحتباس بالموت، فيسترد العوض بعينه إن كان قائما، وقيمته إن كان مستهلكاً كما في سائر الأعواض. والجواب عنه من قبلهما : إن وجوب النفقة وإن كان بالاحتباس لكنها ليست، كسائر الأعواض؛ ولهذا لو هلكت نفقة معجلة مدة من غير استهلاك، ثمّ مات أحدهما لا يُسْتَرَدُّ شيء منها بالإجماع.
(And an advance payment for a period is not reclaimed if one of them dies before it: i.e., if the maintenance for a period, e.g. six months, is paid in advance, and one of them dies before that period, e.g., if he dies after one month, nothing is reclaimed from it according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad [says:] because it is a gift that has been connected with possession, so the right of return drops by death, as in a gift. According to al-Shafi'i, the maintenance for what has passed is counted, i.e., one month for the wife, and the maintenance for five months is reclaimed; because it is compensation for what she is entitled to due to being withheld. And from this it is known that non-reclaimability is shared between death and divorce, and the author did not mention it because he disliked mentioning it in al-Hidayah. [1] He said: 'because it is a gift... etc.' Its summary: maintenance is a gift in itself, even though it is obligatory as a reward for being withheld, and gifts are owned after possession, and the right of return drops at death. [2] He said: 'as in a gift' — because if the donee owns the gifted thing by possession, then the donor or the donee dies, the donor or his heirs may not reclaim it. [3] He said: 'according to Muhammad' — this is the narration of Ibn Rustom from him, and in another narration from him: if she receives the maintenance for a month or less, nothing is reclaimed from it because it is insignificant, so it is treated as current; what is more than that is reclaimed. [4] He said: 'because it is compensation... etc.' Its summary: maintenance is only obligatory for her upon him due to being withheld, so it is compensation for it. So if one of them dies before the expiry of that period for which her maintenance was advanced, the entitlement becomes void due to the loss of being withheld by death, so the compensation is reclaimed in kind if it remains, or its value if it is consumed, as in other compensations. The answer from the two of them is: even though the obligation of maintenance is due to being withheld, it is not like other compensations; hence if an advanced maintenance for a period perishes without consumption, then one of them dies, nothing is reclaimed from it by consensus.
Source pages p. 548

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on advance payment of maintenance (mu'ajjala) for a fixed period. The Hanafi school has an intra-school disagreement. Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf hold that if maintenance is paid in advance for a period (e.g., six months) and one spouse dies before the period ends, the advanced payment is not reclaimed; it remains the wife's property. They analogize it to a gift (hiba): once delivered, ownership is complete and death does not affect it. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, in a narration from Ibn Rustom, distinguishes: if the amount advanced is for a month or less, it is not reclaimed (deemed trivial); if more than a month, the excess is reclaimed. The Shafi'i view, noted for contrast, treats maintenance as compensation for being withheld (ihtibas), so only the amount for the elapsed time is kept and the rest is reclaimed. The Hanafi commentator provides the reasoning: maintenance is a gift by nature, even if it is obligatory due to ihtibas. Gifts are owned upon possession, and death does not reverse that ownership. The response to the Shafi'i argument is that maintenance, though triggered by ihtibas, is not a standard compensation; evidence is that if the advanced goods perish without being consumed (e.g., ruined by accident), no one reclaims anything by consensus. This confirms that it is not a pure exchange contract.

Key Terms
المعجلة (al-mu'ajjala): Maintenance paid in advance for a specified future period.
الاسترداد (al-istirdad): The act of reclaiming or taking back something that was given.
القبض (al-qabd): Physical possession or receipt, which completes ownership of a gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad's narration from Ibn Rustom, if a husband advances six months of maintenance and dies after one month, what is the ruling?
A) The wife keeps the entire six months.
B) The wife keeps one month and must return the remaining five months.
C) The wife must return the entire six months.
D) The wife keeps three months and returns three months.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi majority treats advanced maintenance as a gift, so it is not reclaimed upon death; only Imam Muhammad reclaims the excess over one month.

Maintenance of the Wife of a Full Slave (Qinn)
Defines 'irs al-qinn and states the ruling on her maintenance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'irs al-qinn, distinguish the qinn from mudabbar and mukatab, and state the Hanafi ruling on the wife's recourse when her slave husband cannot provide maintenance.

Core Idea
Only a full slave (qinn) can be sold for his wife's maintenance; the wife of a mudabbar or mukatab must work instead.

This lesson focuses on the technical definition of 'irs al-qinn (the wife of a full slave) and the specific ruling on her maintenance. You will learn why the wife of a mudabbar or mukatab cannot compel their sale to cover maintenance and must instead earn it herself through sa'aya. This distinction is crucial for understanding Hanafi rulings on maintenance obligations in slave marriages.

Guided Path
1
Parse the term 'irs al-qinn
Identify that 'irs means wife, and qinn means a full slave with no freedom. Understand the difference between qinn, mudabbar, and mukatab.
Qinn can be sold; mudabbar and mukatab cannot.
2
Identify the legal ruling
The passage states that the wife of a qinn cannot force the sale of a mudabbar or mukatab for maintenance. Instead, she must earn it herself (sa'aya).
This is the operative ruling (mufta bihi).
3
Understand the rationale
The reason is that the sale of mudabbar and mukatab is invalid (their status prevents sale), so the only recourse is the wife's own work.
Contrast with the qinn, who can be sold.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونفقة عرس القن [0] قوله : عرس القِن؛ العِرس - بالكسر - : الزوجة كما مرّ، والقن - بكسر القاف وتشديد النون - عند الفقهاء عبارة عمّن لا حريّة فيه بوجه من الوجوه، وأشار به إلى أنه لا يُباع غير القن من المدبّر والمكاتب؛ لعدم صحة بيعهما، بل يلزم عليها السعاية؛ لأداء النفقة. كذا في «البحر».
And the maintenance of the wife of a slave (qinn). [0] He said: 'The wife of a slave (qinn); al-'irs (with kasra) means the wife, as mentioned before, and al-qinn (with kasra on the qaf and shaddah on the nun) in the terminology of the jurists refers to one who has no aspect of freedom. And by this [the author] indicates that other than the qinn, i.e., the mudabbar and the mukatab, are not to be sold [to pay for maintenance], because their sale is not valid; rather, the wife must engage in sa'aya (earn through work) to pay for the maintenance. Thus in al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 549

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'irs al-qinn (wife of a slave) and explains the ruling on her maintenance. 'irs means wife, and qinn is a full slave who has no freedom whatsoever—unlike a mudabbar (slave promised emancipation upon the master's death) or a mukatab (slave under contract to buy his freedom). The key ruling is that if a full slave cannot provide maintenance for his wife, the wife cannot force the sale of a mudabbar or mukatab to recover the maintenance because they cannot be sold (their status prevents it). Instead, the wife must perform sa'aya (work) to earn the maintenance herself. This is a specific ruling limited to full slaves; for mudabbar and mukatab, the wife's recourse is different. The source cited is al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Key Terms
عرس (irs): Wife, spouse.
القن (al-qinn): A full slave who possesses no aspect of freedom, i.e., not a mudabbar or mukatab.
السعاية (al-sa'ayah): Earning by work; here, the wife's effort to earn maintenance if the slave husband cannot provide and cannot be sold.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a mukatab slave cannot provide maintenance for his wife, what is the wife's recourse?
A) She can have the mukatab sold to pay the maintenance.
B) She must work to earn her own maintenance.
C) The master of the mukatab must provide for her.
D) She can divorce the mukatab without any payment.

Key Takeaway
Only a full slave (qinn) can be sold for his wife's nafaqah; the wife of a mudabbar or mukatab must work (sa'aya) to secure her maintenance.

Conditions for Selling a Slave to Pay Maintenance Debts
Ruling with conditions and an illustrative distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the three conditions for selling a slave for maintenance debts and distinguish the repeated sale for maintenance from a single sale for other debts.

Core Idea
A slave can be sold repeatedly for each new maintenance debt because the debt is attached to his person and arises periodically.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary explains when and how a slave may be sold to satisfy his wife's maintenance. It sets out three essential conditions and clarifies that, unlike other debts, maintenance debts allow the slave to be sold multiple times as new amounts arise. The commentator also corrects a common misunderstanding about the nature of the second sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling
The passage begins by stating that a slave may be sold by his master or the judge to pay maintenance. Identify this as the main ruling.
The ruling is conditional.
2
Extract the three conditions
The text lists three conditions for the sale: 1) marriage with master's permission, 2) maintenance prescribed by a judge, 3) master does not ransom the slave. Note each condition and its legal rationale.
3
Understand the repeated sale mechanism
The passage distinguishes between maintenance debts and other debts. For maintenance, the slave can be sold again for each new accumulation. For other debts, only one sale is allowed. Grasp the reason: maintenance arises periodically.
The attachment of debt to the slave's person is key.
4
Analyze the buyer's situation
The text discusses the buyer's knowledge and consent. If the buyer is aware of the debt or later agrees, the sale is valid. Otherwise, the slave can be returned as defective. Understand this protects the buyer.
5
Evaluate the commentator's critique
The passage ends by criticizing a mistaken view that the second sale is for the remainder of the first debt. The correct view is that it is for a new, separate debt. This is the thamarah (practical consequence) of the difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : يُباع ؛ أي يبيعه سيّده ، ويؤدّي من ثمنه النفقة ؛ لأنها دين تعلّق برقبته، فيؤمر ببيعه فإن امتنع . عنه باعه القاضي، وهذا مشروط بشروط : أحدهما : أن يكون نكاح العبد بإذن السيد، فإنّه إذا تزوج الفن أو المدبر ونحوهما بلا إذن مولاه فلا نفقة عليه ولا مهر ؛ لعدم صحة النكاح، فإذا أعتق أحدهم جازَ نکاحه حين عتق ، فيجب عليه المهرُ والنفقة في المستقبل، ويطالب هو بهما. وبالجملة : لا بيع في هذه الصورة. وثانيها : أن تكون النفقةُ مفروضة فرضها القاضي ؛ لأنها بدون الفرض تسقط بالمضي: كنفقة زوجة الحرّ، وقد أشار الشارح له إلى هذين الشرطين في تصوير المسألة. وثالثها: أن لا يختار المولى فداءه، فإن اختار المولى ذلك فلا يُباع ؛ لأن حقها إنما هو في النفقة لا في الرقبة. كذا في «النهر»، و«الفتح». 
قوله : مرة بعد أخرى ؛ يعني إذا اجتمعت عليه نفقة مفروضة يُباع فيها ، ثم إذا اجتمعت عليه نفقةٌ يُباع مرّة أخرى وهكذا ، وهذا إذا عَلِمَ المشتري أنّ عليه. أو لم يعلم ، ثم علم فرضي به.
دين النفقة وأما إذا لم يعلم بحاله أو علم بعد الشراء ولم يرض فله رده ؛ لأنه عيب اطلع عليه، وإنما يباع مرة أخرى ؛ لأنَّ النفقة تجب شيئا فشيئاً، فالواجب مرة أخرى دين حادث وجب عليه، فيباع فيه وهكذا. كذا في «الفتح».
وفي دين غيرها يباع مرة، صورتُه : عبد تزوج امرأة بإذن المولى، ففرض القاضي النفقة عليه، فاجتمع عليه ألف درهم، فبيع بخمسمئة، وهي قيمته، والمشتري عالم أن عليه دينُ النفقة يباعُ مرَّةً أخرى بخلاف ما إذا كان هذا الألفُ عليه بسبب آخر، فبيع بخمسمئة لا يباع مرة أخرى.

وأما ما ذكره الشارح ههاهنا من تصوير البيع مرة بعد أخرى وتبعه صاحب «الدرر شرح الغرر) فلا يخلو عن تقصير وسهو ؛ لأنه يؤذن بأنّ بيعه ثانياً إنّما هو فيما بقي عليه من الأوّل وليس كذلك، بل يُباع ثانياً فيما وجب عليه ثانياً بعد أداء الأول.
His statement: 'is sold' means his master sells him and pays the maintenance from its price, because it is a debt attached to his person. He is ordered to sell, and if he refuses, the judge sells him. This is conditional upon conditions: First: that the slave's marriage was with the master's permission, for if he—the qinn or mudabbar etc.—married without his master's permission, there is no maintenance or dowry upon him due to the invalidity of the marriage. If one of them is freed, his marriage becomes valid upon emancipation, and future dowry and maintenance become obligatory, and he is held responsible for them. In short, there is no sale in this case. Second: that the maintenance is prescribed by the judge, because without prescription it lapses with the passage of time, like the maintenance of a free wife's husband. The commentator has indicated these two conditions in framing the issue. Third: that the master does not choose to ransom him; if the master chooses that, then he is not sold, because her right is in maintenance, not in the person. Thus in 'al-Nahr' and 'al-Fath'. 
His statement: 'once after another' means that if prescribed maintenance accumulates upon him, he is sold for it; then if another accumulation occurs, he is sold again, and so on. This is the case if the buyer knows that he owes a maintenance debt. Or if he did not know, then knew and was satisfied with it. But if he did not know his situation, or knew after purchase and was not satisfied, then he has the right to return him because it is a defect he discovered. He is only sold again because maintenance becomes due bit by bit, so what becomes due another time is a newly incurred debt, so he is sold for it, and so on. Thus in 'al-Fath'. 
And for debts other than this, he is sold once. Its scenario: a slave married a woman with the master's permission, the judge prescribed maintenance upon him, and one thousand dirhams accumulated upon him. He was sold for five hundred, which is his value, and the buyer knows that he has a maintenance debt; he is sold another time. Contrary to if this thousand was due for another reason, and he was sold for five hundred, he is not sold another time. 
As for what the commentator mentioned here about depicting sale once after another, and followed by the author of 'al-Durar Sharh al-Ghurur', it is not free from deficiency and oversight, because it suggests that his second sale is only for what remains of the first, but it is not so. Rather, he is sold a second time for what became obligatory upon him a second time after payment of the first.
Source pages p. 550

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (dalil) regarding the sale of a slave to satisfy his wife's maintenance debt. The commentator explains three conditions for such a sale: 1) The marriage must have been with the master's permission; otherwise, the marriage is invalid and no maintenance is due. 2) The maintenance amount must be prescribed by a judge; otherwise, unpaid maintenance is forfeited with time. 3) The master does not choose to ransom the slave. If these conditions are met, the slave may be sold repeatedly for each new accumulation of maintenance, unlike other debts for which he is sold only once. The reason is that maintenance arises periodically. The commentator also critiques a certain understanding that the second sale is for the remainder of the first debt, clarifying it is for a new debt. This ruling is from 'al-Fath' (Fath al-Qadir).

Key Terms
تعلّق برقبته (ta'allaqa bi-raqabatihi): The debt is attached to the slave's person, making him liable to be sold.
فداء (fidaa): Ransoming the slave by the master, which prevents sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when can a slave be sold again after already being sold for a maintenance debt?
A) For the remaining balance of the first debt if not fully paid
B) For any new accumulation of prescribed maintenance after the first debt was paid
C) Only if the master approves a second sale
D) Never; maintenance debts are sold only once

Key Takeaway
A slave may be sold repeatedly for each new prescribed maintenance debt, unlike other fixed debts which allow only one sale.

The Wife's Right to a Separate Dwelling
Ruling on the wife's right to separate housing with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the wife's separate dwelling, cite the Quranic proof, and identify the exceptions for the husband's slave-girl and very young child.

Core Idea
The wife's dwelling must be free of the husband's family to prevent harm.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the ruling that a wife is entitled to a separate dwelling, not shared with the husband's relatives. The obligation is derived from the Quranic verses on lodging and the prohibition of harm. The commentator also clarifies narrow exceptions, such as the husband's slave-girl and a very young child.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
The ruling is that the husband must provide a separate dwelling for his wife. The basis is Quran 65:6: 'Lodge them where you lodge according to your means.' This indicates obligation like maintenance.
2
Understand the condition of the house
The house must not contain any member of the husband's family. This is derived from the prohibition in the same verse: 'Do not harm them.' Living with family causes harm.
Harm includes inability to relax, socialize, and enjoy intimacy freely.
3
Note the exceptions
Two exceptions: (1) The husband's slave-girl may reside with the wife because he needs her services. (2) A very young child who does not understand intercourse may also be housed with the wife.
The slave-girl is owned by the husband, so her service is always needed.
4
Recognize the scope of 'family'
The term includes all close relatives and dependents, even the umm walad (mother of his child). The key is that their presence leads to estrangement and harm.
Umm walad is a slave who bore the husband's child.
5
Connect to the principle of preventing harm
The entire ruling is built on the Quranic command not to harm the wife. Separate housing is a means to fulfill that command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ويجب؛ الأصلُ في وجوب المسكن قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم في سورة الطلاق: أَسْكُنُوهُنَّ مِنْ حَيْثُ سَكَنتُم مِّن وُجدكُمْ ) (۲) : أي طاقتكم، فيكون وجوب السكني كوجوب النفقة حالهما يساراً وإعساراً.
(۲) قوله : في بيت ؛ سواء كان ذلك ملكاً له أو إجارة أو عارية.
[٣] قوله : ليس فيه ؛ الجملة صفة لبيت، والأصل فيه قوله ما بعد آية السكني : وَلَا تُضَارُوهُنَّ لِتُضَيْقُوا عَلَيْهِنَّ (٣) ، فإنّه نهى عن إضرار الزوجة، والبيت إذا كان فيها أحد من أهله تتضرر به من حيث أنها لا تقدر على الانبساط التام والمعاشرة والاستمتاع مع الزوج كلّما أرادت ، ولا تأمن على متاعها وغيره.
[٤] قوله : أحد من أهله؛ أي من أهل الزوج ، يعني أعزّته ومتعلّقيه حتى أمّ الولد ، لما أنّ معيّتها مورثة إلى الوحشة والمضرّة ، وأما الأمة ؛ أي أمة الزوج فله إسكانها معها ؛ لأنه يحتاج إلى استخدامها كل حين. كذا في «المحيط» و«الفتح»(4).
(01) قوله : ولو الواو واصلة؛ أي ولو كان ولد الزوج من زوجة أخرى ، ؛ ويستثنى منه طفله الذي لا يفهم الجماع، وما يتعلق به، فلا ضير في معيته.
His statement: 'and it is obligatory' – the basis for the obligation of housing is His statement (peace be upon him) in Surah al-Talaq: 'Lodge them where you lodge according to your means' (65:6): i.e., your capacity. Thus the obligation of housing is like the obligation of maintenance, in terms of both ease and hardship. (2) His statement: 'in a house' – whether it is owned by him, rented, or borrowed. (3) His statement: 'in which there is not' – the clause is an adjective for house, and its basis is the statement after the housing verse: 'And do not harm them to make difficulties for them' (65:6), for He forbade harming the wife. If the house has any of his family in it, she is harmed because she cannot fully relax, socialize, and enjoy intimacy with her husband whenever she wishes, nor is she safe for her belongings and other things. (4) His statement: 'anyone from his family' – meaning from the husband's family, i.e., his close relatives and dependents, even his umm walad, because her company leads to estrangement and harm. As for the slave-girl – i.e., the husband's slave-girl – he may house her with the wife, because he needs her services at all times. Thus in 'al-Muhit' and 'al-Fath'. (5) His statement: 'even if' – the waw is connecting; i.e., even if it is the husband's child from another wife. An exception is made for his young child who does not understand intercourse and what pertains to it, so there is no harm in his company.
Source pages p. 551

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the wife's right to a separate dwelling. The commentator cites Quranic verses: 'Lodge them where you lodge' (65:6) and 'Do not harm them' (65:6) as the basis. The wife must be housed in a dwelling that does not contain any member of the husband's family, because their presence causes harm and restricts her freedom. The term 'his family' includes all close relatives, including his umm walad (the mother of his child). An exception is made for the husband's slave-girl, who may reside with the wife because the husband needs her for service. Another exception is made for a very young child who is not yet aware of marital relations; his presence does not cause harm. This ruling is from 'al-Muhit' and 'al-Fath'.

Key Terms
مِنْ وُجْدِكُمْ (min wujdikum): According to your means or capacity, used in the verse to set the standard for housing.
أمة (amah): A female slave owned by the husband; may be housed with the wife due to the need for her service.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following may reside with the wife in her dwelling according to the Hanafi school?
A) The husband's mother
B) The husband's adult son from another wife
C) The husband's slave-girl
D) The husband's sister

Key Takeaway
A husband must provide a dwelling separate from his family to avoid harm to the wife, with only narrow exceptions for his slave-girl and very young child.

Wife's Right to a Separate Locked Room
Commentary on matn about wife's accommodation requirement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum accommodation requirement for a wife in Hanafi fiqh and explain why a single separate locked room with private facilities suffices.

Core Idea
A single separate locked room with private kitchen and toilet suffices the wife; she cannot demand a whole separate house.

This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah clarifies the wife's right to accommodation. The matn states that a separate locked room is sufficient. The commentary expands on what makes a room truly separate and what facilities must be provided, drawing on earlier Hanafi sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the obligation and its minimum
The husband is obliged to provide accommodation. The matn states that a single separate room with a lock suffices. Understand that this is the minimum requirement; wealthier wives may require more.
The obligation is wajib, not fard, because it is derived from maintenance (nafaqah) which is based on zanni evidence.
2
Understand the condition of separation and lock
The room must be separate (mufrad) from other rooms, isolated, and have a lock (ghalaq) for privacy and security. The commentary from al-Bazzaziyya and al-Bada'i' confirms that if the husband provides such a room, the wife cannot demand another.
The lock must be operable with a key, not just a latch.
3
Review the hierarchy of dwellings
The commentary defines three terms: dar (complete dwelling with courtyard), manzil (rooms without courtyard), and bayt (single roofed structure). The wife is entitled to a bayt, not necessarily a manzil or dar.
This hierarchy helps determine if the accommodation is adequate.
