ولده من غيرها إلا برضاها، وبيت مفرد من دار له غلق
[١]قوله : إلا برضاها ؛ استثناء من قوله : «ليس فيه أحد.... الخ، يعني إن رضيت بقيام أهله فيه فهو جائز ؛ لأنها رضيت بنقص حقها.
[٢]قوله : وبيت مفرد...الخ ؛ حاصله أنه يكفي لها بيت واحدٌ من دار مشتمل على بيوت كثيرة، بشرط أن يكون مفرداً عن البيوت الآخر؛ أي منحازا ومنفصلا عنها، بحيث لا تتضرر بمرور سكّان البيوت الأخر عليها، وتقتدر على حفظ متاعها، وقضاء حوائجها.
قال في «البزازية»: أبت أن تسكن مع أحماء الزوج وفي الدارِ بيوت إن فرّغ لها بيتاً له غلق على حدة، وليس فيه أحدٌ منهم لا تتمكن من مطالبته ببيت آخر. انتهى. وفي «البدائع»: لو أراد أن يسكنها مع ضرتها أو مع أحمائها كأمه وأخته وبنته ، فأبت فعليه أن يسكنها في منزل منفرد ؛ لأنّ إباءها دليلُ الأذى والضرر ؛ ولأنه يحتاج إلى جماعها ومعاشرتها في أي وقت يتفق ، ولا يمكن ذلك مع ثالث، حتى لو كان في الدارِ بيوت وجعل لبيتها غلقا على حدة، قالوا ليس لها أن تطالبه بآخر. انتهى.
واعلم أن هاهنا ثلاثة ألفاظ مستعملة في عباراتهم بيت، ومنزل، ودار، أعلاها : آخرها، وأدناها أولها ، وأوسطها أوسطها ، فقد نقل والدي العلام أدخله الله دار السلام في «شرح الهداية» المسمّى «السقاية لعطشان الهداية»: عن «المجتبى»: إنّ الدار اسم لما يشتمل على الصحن والبيوت والصفّة والمطبخ والاصطبل، والمنزل : ما يشتمل على بيوت ومطبخ ومستراح دون الصحن، والبيت : اسم لمسقف واحد له دهليز انتهى.
[٣] قوله : له غلق ؛ الجملة صفة لبيت، كما أنّ قوله : «مفرد» صفة، والغلق بفتحتين : ما يغلق ويفتح بالمفتاح، ويقال له : القفل، وذكر في «الفتح» و«البحر» : إنّه ينبغي أن يكون المطبخ والكنيف داخل البيت أو في الدار ، ولا يشاركها أحد فيهما من أهل الدار ؛ لأن المقصود لا يحصل إلا به.
كفاها ، وله منع والديها وولدها من غيره من الدُّخُول عليها، لا من النظر إليها، وكلامها متى شاءوا كفاها
[١] قوله : كفاها ؛ خبر لقوله : وبيت، وفيه إشارة إلى أنّ هذا بيان الله ، وفي لأوساط الناس، وأمّا في الأغنياء والشريفة فلا يكفي ذلك، فيجب عليه إسكانها حسبما يناسب حاله وحالهما.
[٢] قوله : وله منع ... الخ ؛ أي يجوز له أن يمنع والدي زوجتها وأولادها من غيره، وكذا غيرهم من أقاربها من الدخول عليها في بيته، نعم لو قاموا على باب الدار لا بأس به، وهذا أحد الأقوال في المسألة.
والقول الثاني : إنّه لا يمنع من الدخول مطلقاً ، وإنما يمنع من القرار والمكث فيه. والقول الثالث : إنّه لا يمنع من دخول الوالدين في بيته في كل جمعة مرة، ومن دخول غيرهما في كلّ سنة مرّة، ذكر صاحب الهداية» هذه الأقوال، وصحح الأخير.
[٣] قوله : ملکه ؛ المراد به أعم من الملكِ الحقيقي ؛ ليشمل ما إذا أسكنها في بيت إجارة أو إعارة.
[٤] قوله : فله المنع ؛ قد يعارض ذلك بأنه لما أسكنها في ذلك البيت قضاء لما هو الواجب عليها تعلق حقها به فلا يجوز له ما تتضرّر به المرأة وتحزن، وهو منع دخول والديها عليها.
[٥] قوله : لا ؛ أي ليس له منعها من ملاقاة والديها ونظرهما إليها، وكلامها معهم متى شاؤوا ؛ لأنّ في هذا المنع قطيعة رح رحم، ومضارة بينة.
His child from another wife except with her consent, and a separate room in a house with a lock suffices her. [1] His statement: 'except with her consent' – an exception from his statement: 'there is no one in it...' etc., meaning if she consents to his family living in it, it is permissible because she consented to a reduction of her right. [2] His statement: 'and a separate room...' etc. – its summary: a single room from a house containing many rooms suffices her, on condition that it is separate from the other rooms, i.e., isolated and detached from them, such that she is not harmed by the passage of residents of the other rooms over her, and she is able to store her belongings and attend to her needs. He stated in al-Bazzaziyya: 'If she refuses to live with the husband's in-laws and the house has rooms, if he vacates a room for her with a separate lock, and none of them are in it, she cannot demand another room.' End quote. And in al-Bada'i': 'If he wants to house her with her co-wife or with his in-laws like his mother, sister, and daughter, and she refuses, then he must house her in a separate dwelling because her refusal indicates harm and injury, and because he needs to have intercourse and companionship with her at any time that occurs, and that is not possible with a third party. Even if the house has rooms and he makes her room have a separate lock, they said she cannot demand another.' End quote. Know that here there are three terms used in their expressions: bayt, manzil, and dar. The highest is the last, the lowest is the first, and the middle is the middle. My father, the scholar (may Allah enter him into the abode of peace), narrated in his commentary on al-Hidayah named al-Siqayah li-Atashan al-Hidayah, from al-Mujtaba: 'Dar is a name for what includes the courtyard, rooms, portico, kitchen, and stable; manzil is what includes rooms, kitchen, and bathroom without the courtyard; bayt is a name for a single roofed structure with a vestibule.' End quote. [3] His statement: 'with a lock' – the sentence is an adjective for bayt, just as his statement 'separate' is an adjective. Al-ghalaq (with two fatha) is what is locked and opened with a key, also called al-qufl. It is mentioned in al-Fath and al-Bahr: 'It is recommended that the kitchen and toilet be inside the room or in the courtyard, and no one from the house residents should share them with her because the intended purpose cannot be achieved without that.' It suffices her, and he may prevent her parents and his child from others entering upon her, but not from looking at her and speaking to her whenever they wish; it suffices her. [1] His statement: 'it suffices her' – predicate of his statement 'and a room', and it indicates that this is an explanation for the middle class of people. As for the wealthy and the noble, that does not suffice; he must house her in a manner befitting his and their status. [2] His statement: 'and he may prevent...' etc. – meaning it is permissible for him to prevent the parents of his wife and his child from others, and likewise other relatives of hers, from entering upon her in his house. Yes, if they stand at the door of the house, there is no harm. This is one of the statements on the issue. The second statement: he does not prevent entry absolutely; rather, he prevents staying and settling. The third statement: he does not prevent the entry of parents into his house once every Friday, and the entry of others once every year. The author of al-Hidayah mentioned these statements and authenticated the last. [3] His statement: 'his property' – what is meant is more general than actual ownership, to include when he houses her in a rented or borrowed house. [4] His statement: 'so he may prevent' – this may be countered by the fact that when he housed her in that house to fulfill what is obligatory upon him, her right became attached to it, so it is not permissible for him to do what harms and saddens her, which is preventing her parents from entering upon her. [5] His statement: 'not' – i.e., he may not prevent her from meeting her parents, them looking at her, and her speaking with them whenever they wish, because in such prevention there is severance of ties of kinship and clear harm.
Source pages p. 552

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn regarding the wife's right to accommodation and the husband's authority over visitors. It covers two main rulings. First, the requirement for a separate locked room: if the wife consents to living with the husband's family, it is permissible; otherwise, she is entitled to a room separate from others, with a lock, and with private access to kitchen and toilet. The commentary cites al-Bazzaziyya and al-Bada'i' to confirm that a single separate room suffices, provided it is isolated, and the wife cannot demand a separate house if the room meets these conditions. It also defines the terms 'dar', 'manzil', and 'bayt' to clarify the levels of accommodation. Second, the husband may prevent the wife's relatives (parents and children from other marriages) from entering the house, but he cannot prevent them from seeing her or speaking with her, as that would constitute severance of kinship ties. The commentary notes three scholarly positions on this, and the author of al-Hidayah prefers the view that parents may visit weekly and others yearly. Key points: the wife's consent to a lesser accommodation is valid; a separate room with a lock is the minimum; the husband's property right allows him to exclude visitors, but not to the extent of preventing permissible seeing and speaking.

Key Terms
غلق (ghalaq): A lock or latch that can be opened with a key; used as a condition for the wife's room to ensure privacy and security.
دار (dār): A complete dwelling including courtyard (sahn), rooms (buyūt), portico (ṣuffa), kitchen (maṭbakh), and stable (iṣṭabl).
بيت (bayt): A single roofed structure with a vestibule (dihliz); the basic unit of accommodation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the minimum accommodation that suffices for a wife according to the Hanafi school as stated in this passage?
A) A whole separate house with multiple rooms
B) A single room in a house with a separate lock
C) A room without a lock but with a separate entrance
D) A shared room with the husband's family

Key Takeaway
A single separate locked room with private facilities is the minimum wajib accommodation for a wife in Hanafi fiqh.

Husband's Authority to Prevent Entry of Wife's Relatives
Commentary on matn about husband's authority over visitors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the husband's authority to prevent entry of the wife's relatives and identify the limits according to the preferred Hanafi position.

Core Idea
The husband may prevent entry of his wife's relatives, but cannot prevent them from seeing or speaking to her; the preferred view allows weekly visits for parents and yearly for others.

This passage continues the commentary on the matn, addressing the husband's right to control who enters his house. The matn states that he may prevent the wife's parents and children from entering, but not from seeing or speaking. The commentary presents three scholarly positions and selects the most balanced one.

Guided Path
1
State the husband's right to prevent entry
The matn gives the husband authority to prevent the wife's parents and children (from other marriages) from entering his house. This is based on his property rights and the need for privacy.
2
Identify the three positions
The commentary lists three views: (1) absolute prevention of entry; (2) prevention of staying but not entering; (3) prevention limited: parents may enter once a week, others once a year.
The author of al-Hidayah preferred the third position.
3
Understand the limits
Even though he may prevent entry, he cannot prevent them from seeing her or speaking with her whenever they wish, because that would sever family ties and cause harm.
Standing at the door is also not prohibited.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn regarding the wife's right to accommodation and the husband's authority over visitors. It covers two main rulings. Key Terms
أحماء (aḥmā’): Relatives of the husband, specifically his parents, siblings, and children from other marriages.
منع الدخول (manʿ al-dukhūl): Preventing entry into the house; the husband's right to control who enters his property.

intermediate

Husband's Right to Prevent Relatives and Preferred Visits
Explains husband's right to prevent entry and the preferred Hanafi ruling on visits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi opinions on the frequency of visits by the wife's relatives and identify the mufta bihi opinion.

Core Idea
Husband cannot prevent wife from seeing her parents weekly; other mahrams yearly.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies the husband's authority over his wife's visitors. While the husband owns the house, the wife's right to maintain family ties limits his power. The commentary presents three Hanafi opinions, with the third being the relied-upon view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on preventing entry
The text states that the husband may prevent entry because the house is his property, but he may not prevent looking or speaking.
2
Distinguish between preventing entry vs. seeing
The husband's right concerns entry into the house, not the relatives' ability to see or speak to the wife outside.
This distinction is crucial.
3
State the three Hanafi opinions
First: absolute prevention of entry. Second: no prevention of entry but restriction of long stays. Third: wife may go out to parents and they may enter weekly; other mahrams yearly.
These opinions are from al-Hidayah.
4
Explain the tarjih reasoning
The third opinion is preferred to avoid harm and severance of kinship (qati'ah al-rahim).
The author of al-Hidayah deems it correct.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The operative ruling for fatwa is the third opinion: parents weekly, other mahrams yearly.
Fatwa is upon this view.

He has the right to prevent her parents and her children from others from entering upon her, not from looking at her, and their speaking to her whenever they wish. ... And it is said: She is not prevented from going out to the parents, nor from their entering upon her every Friday, and for a mahram other than them every year; it is the correct opinion, and the fatwa is upon it.
Source pages p. 553

Commentary
This passage discusses the husband's rights regarding visitors to his wife in the marital home. The core ruling is that the husband can prevent anyone from entering his house, including the wife's parents and children from a previous marriage, but he cannot prevent them from seeing her or speaking with her. The commentary presents three opinions within the Hanafi school: (1) absolute right to prevent entry, but no issue if they stand at the door; (2) cannot prevent entry entirely, only long stays; (3) the wife cannot be prevented from going out to her parents or their entry once a week, and other mahram relatives once a year. The author of al-Hidayah deems the third opinion correct, and the fatwa is based on it. The commentary also clarifies that 'house' includes rented or borrowed property, and the wife's right to see her parents is protected to avoid severing family ties.

Key Terms
محرَم (mahram): A person whom one is permanently prohibited to marry due to blood, marriage, or suckling relationship.
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance including food, clothing, and housing.
قطيعة الرحم (qati'ah al-rahim): Severing family ties, a major sin in Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Once every week
D) At any time without restriction

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi requires the husband to allow the wife's parents to visit at least once a week and other mahrams once a year.

Judge Fixes Maintenance for Absent Husband
Rules on judge fixing maintenance for wife of absent husband

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a judge may fix maintenance for the wife of an absent husband and identify the three required types of maintenance.

Core Idea
Even a short absence qualifies: the judge can fix maintenance for the absent husband's wife.

This lesson clarifies the Hanafi ruling on maintenance for a wife whose husband is absent. The passage expands the scope of 'absent' to include even brief trips, ensuring the wife's right to food, clothing, and shelter is protected. Understanding this ruling prevents hardship and illustrates the judge's role in enforcing family obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its hukm level
The judge fixes maintenance (nafaqah) for the wife. The ruling is that the judge may (wajib when necessary) fix maintenance to prevent hardship.
The husband's obligation to provide maintenance is wajib; the judge enforces it.
2
Define the maintenance
The text specifies three types: food (ṭa‘ām), clothing (kiswa), and housing (suknā). These are the minimum required.
Other schools may include medical expenses, but Hanafi limits to these three.
3
Determine the scope of 'absent'
The term 'ghā’ib' includes the missing (mafqūd) and those on short trips, even if the husband went to a nearby village. The absence does not need to be long.
This is the key expansion: even a very short absence qualifies.
4
Note the implicit condition
The wife must have no access to her husband's funds during his absence. If she has access, the judge does not intervene.
This condition is derived from the purpose of preventing hardship.
5
Recognize the authority
The ruling is cited from al-Muḥīṭ, a reliable Hanafi source in the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah tradition.
This adds authority weight: Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويفرض نفقة عرس الغائب. قوله : ويفرض نفقة... الخ؛ المراد بالنفقة أنواعها الثلاثة من: الطعام، والكسوة، والسكني، وفي إطلاق الغائب إشارة إلى شمول الحكم للمفقود ولغيره، ولما إذا كانت الغيبة أقل من مدّة سفر، حتى لو ذهب إلى قرية وتركها في البلد، فللقاضي أن يفرض لها النفقة. كذا في «المحيط».
The judge fixes maintenance for the wife of an absent husband. ... The maintenance includes its three types: food, clothing, and housing. In the general term 'absent' there is indication of inclusion of the missing person and others, and also when the absence is less than travel distance, even if he went to a village and left her in the city, the judge may fix maintenance for her. As in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 554

Commentary
This passage addresses the situation when a husband is absent and not providing maintenance. The Hanafi school holds that the judge may fix maintenance for the wife from the husband's property, or if he has none, from the husband's debt. The maintenance covers food, clothing, and shelter. The ruling applies whether the husband is missing (mafqud) or merely away on a short trip. The key condition is that the wife has no access to her husband's funds. This is based on the general principle that the husband must provide maintenance, and if he fails, the judge can enforce it.

Key Terms
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance covering food, clothing, and housing.
غائب (gha'ib): A husband who is absent from the marital home, including missing (mafqud) or on a trip.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Food, shelter, and education
B) Food, clothing, and housing
C) Medical, housing, and transport
D) Food, jewellery, and housing

Key Takeaway
The judge may fix maintenance (food, clothing, housing) for the wife of any absent husband, even if the absence is very short.

Husband May Not Prevent Wife from Visiting Parents and Mahram
Ruling on wife's visitation rights with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a wife's visitation rights to her parents and mahram, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and apply the mufta bihi condition.

Core Idea
Wife may visit parents weekly and mahram yearly; husband cannot prevent unless he can visit himself.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya addresses a common family issue: a husband's right to restrict his wife's travel. While classical Hanafi law upholds a wife's right to visit her parents and mahram regularly, it also acknowledges the potential for fitna. The commentator navigates between the absolute ruling and practical safeguards, favoring the view of Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and general ruling
The act is the wife's visitation to her parents and other mahram. The general ruling is that the husband cannot prevent it.
Note that 'parents' includes both husband's and wife's parents.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is the hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) allowed a foster uncle to enter upon his wives, showing that mahram, including non-blood relatives, cannot be hindered.
This is a dalil naqli from Sunnah ahad, referenced in Bukhari.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's permission for the foster uncle indicates that a mahram (by rada'ah) may visit without prevention. The same principle applies to parents and all mahram.
The inclusion of foster relatives is a specific Hanafi point.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf qualified the ruling: the wife may only go if the husband is unable to visit the parents/mahram himself. Abu Hanifa gave an absolute right.
Some scholars even opted to prevent, but the text rejects that.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The text adopts Abu Yusuf's view as mufta bihi (operative for fatwa) when the parents are as described, to avoid fitna. Frequent visits may be restricted if they cause trouble.
The tarjih authority is al-Fath (4:398).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل : لا تمنع من الخروج إلى الوالدين، ولا من دخولهما عليها كل جمعة، وفي محرم غيرهما كل سنة، هو الصحيح، وعليه الفتوى.

[1] قوله : إلى الوالدين؛ أي إلى بيتهما لملاقاتهما، وأما الأجانب فله المنع من زيارتهم وعيادتهم، وله المنع من الشركة في مجالس الوليمة وغيرها مما تحتمل فيه الفتنة احتمالاً غالباً.

[2] قوله : كل جمعة؛ ليس المراد به يوم الجمعة المعين، بل المراد به دورة السبع؛ أي في سبعة أيام مرة في أي يوم كان.

[3] قوله : غيرهما؛ صفة لمحرم؛ أي في محرم لها غير الوالدين لا يمنع عن خروجها إليه ودخوله عليها في كل سنة مرة، وفي الإطلاق إشارة إلى شمول الحكم للمحرم غير ذي رحم أيضاً: كالأخ الرضاعي والعم الرضاعي. ولذا لم يمنع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عن دخول العم الرضاعي لزوجته حفصة رضي الله عنها، والعم الرضاعي لزوجته عائشة رضي الله عنها، بل أجاز له على ما أخرجه البخاري وغيره من أصحاب الصحاح.

وعن أبي يوسف تقييد خروجها بأن لا يقدر على إتيانها، وهو حسن وقد اختار بعض المشايخ منعها من الخروج إليهما، والحق الأخذ بقول أبي يوسف إذا كان الأبوان بالصفة المذكورة، وإن لم يكونا كذلك ينبغي أن يأذن لها في زيارتهما في الحين بعد الحين على قدر متعارف، أما في كل جمعة فهو بعيد فإن في كثرة الخروج فتح باب الفتنة خصوصاً الشابة والزوج من ذوي الهيئات، وحيث أبحنا لها الخروج فإنما يباح بشرط عدم الزينة وتغيير الهيئة إلى ما يكون داعية لنظر الرجال والاستمالة. «الفتح»(٤ : ٣٩٨). (۲) ينظر: «شرح ملا مسكين (ص) (۱۳۳)، و(الدر المتنقى» (۱): ٤٩٣)، وفي «الاختيار» (۳: ۲۳۹) : وهو المختار
And it is said: She is not prevented from going out to the parents, nor from their entering upon her every Friday, and regarding a mahram other than them, every year; this is the correct, and upon it is the fatwa. [1] His statement: 'to the parents' means to their house to meet them. As for strangers, he may prevent her from visiting them or attending their sick visitation, and he may prevent her from participating in feasts and other gatherings where fitna is likely. [2] His statement: 'every Friday' does not mean the specific day of Friday, but rather the cycle of seven days, i.e., once in seven days regardless of the day. [3] His statement: 'other than them' is an adjective for mahram; meaning that for a mahram other than the parents, he is not prevented from going out to him or his entering upon her once every year. The absolute expression indicates the inclusion of mahram who are not blood relatives as well, such as a foster brother and foster uncle. For this reason, the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) did not prevent the foster uncle from entering upon his wife Hafsa (may Allah be pleased with her), and the foster uncle from entering upon his wife Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her), but rather permitted him, as recorded by Bukhari and other compilers of authentic hadith. And from Abu Yusuf: a restriction that she may go out only if he is unable to come to her. This is good, and some scholars have chosen to prevent her from going out to them. The correct opinion is to adopt the view of Abu Yusuf when the parents are as described. If they are not, then he should allow her to visit them occasionally in a customary manner. As for every Friday, that is far-fetched, for frequent going out opens the door to fitna, especially for a young woman and a husband of standing. Where we permit her to go out, it is only permitted on condition that she does not adorn herself or change her appearance to attract the gaze of men and their inclination. From al-Fath (4:398).
Source pages p. 554

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the wife's right to visit her parents and other mahram relatives. The commentator explains that the husband cannot prevent the wife from visiting her parents at their home once a week, nor from them visiting her. The phrase 'every Friday' means once every seven days, not specifically Friday. For other mahram relatives, such as siblings or in-laws through breastfeeding, the visit is allowed once a year. The proof for including non-blood mahram is the hadith that the Prophet allowed the rada'ah (foster) uncle to visit his wives. The commentator then mentions a difference among Hanafi scholars: Abu Yusuf added a condition that the wife may only go out if the husband cannot come to her himself. The preferred view (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Yusuf when the parents are as described. However, if the parents are not such, then permission should be given occasionally. The commentator notes that frequent outings, especially weekly, could lead to fitna, so permission is conditional on modesty.

Key Terms
الوالدين (al-walidayn): The parents of the wife.
محرم (mahram): A person who is permanently prohibited from marrying the wife due to blood, marriage, or fosterage.
كل جمعة (kull jumuʿah): Every week (not specifically Friday).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How often may a wife visit her parents according to the text?
A) Every day
B) Once a week
C) Once a year
D) As she wishes

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi position is that a wife may not be prevented from visiting her parents weekly and mahram yearly, but if the husband can visit them himself, he may prevent her.

Conditions for Fixing Maintenance from Absentee's Property
Ruling on conditions for imposing nafaqah from absentee's property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a Hanafi judge may fix maintenance from an absentee's property and identify which recipients are eligible.

Core Idea
A judge can only use the absentee's property of the same type as maintenance right for wife, child, and parents.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the precise conditions for a judge to extract maintenance for the family of an absent husband. It restricts the ruling to certain recipients, certain types of property, and certain forms of holding the property. The reasoning (talil) is that for some relatives, maintenance is already obligatory before any court order, so the judge merely assists; for others, a court order would create a new obligation, which is not allowed against an absentee.

Guided Path
1
Identify Eligible Recipients
List the persons for whom maintenance can be fixed: wife, child (free poor, including infirm adult and poor female), and parents (poor and needy). Note that siblings and other mahrams are excluded.
The text explicitly contrasts with brother and mahram relatives.
2
Identify Permissible Property Type
The property must be of the same genus as the maintenance right, i.e., food, clothing, dirhams, or dinars. If the property consists of goods that need to be sold, maintenance cannot be fixed because selling an absentee's property is not allowed.
Key phrase: 'min jins haqqihim'.
3
Identify Permissible Forms of Property
The property must be in one of three forms: deposit (wadiah), a debt owed to the absentee, or mudaraba capital. This ensures the property is readily accessible without new disposition.
The text specifies 'inda mudi' (deposit), 'dayn' (debt), or 'mudaraba'.
4
Understand the Rationale (Talil)
For wife, child, and parents, maintenance is wajib even before a judge's decree; they can take from his property if they find it. Thus the judge's order is mere assistance. For others, maintenance is not wajib before decree, so the judge would be imposing a new obligation, which is not permissible on an absentee.
Contrast between 'initial imposition' and 'assistance'.
5
Recognize the Prohibition on Sale
The judge cannot order the sale of the absentee's goods to provide maintenance because selling an absentee's property without his consent is impermissible according to the Hanafi school.
This is a distinctive Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويفرض نفقة وطفله وأبويه في مال له من جنس حقهم فقط عند مودع.

وفي الاقتصار على فرض نفقة الزوجة والأبوين والطفل إشارة إلى أنه لا يفرض في مال الغائب نفقة غيرهم كأخيه، وكل ذي رحم محرم سوى قرابة الولاد. والوجه في ذلك: أن نفقتهم لا تجب قبل القضاء؛ ولهذا ليس لهم أن يأخذوا شيئاً من ملكه إذا ظفروا به، فكان القضاء في حقهم ابتداء إيجاب، ولا يجوز في الغائب؛ لعدم جواز القضاء على الغائب عندنا على ما سيأتي في موضعه. بخلاف الزوجة وأهل قرابة الولاد، فإن لهم الأخذ قبل القضاء بلا رضاء، فيكون الفرض في حقهم إعانة لهم لا قضاء، وكذا لا يفرض المملوكه حال غيبوبته. كذا في «الدرر شرح الغرر».

قوله : وطفله؛ أي الفقير الحرّ، وكذا الحكم في الكبير الزمن، والأنثى الفقيرة مطلقاً.

قوله : وأبويه؛ أي الفقيرين المحتاجين إليه على ما سيأتي ذكره.

قوله : من جنس حقهم؛ الحاصل : إن حقهم إنما هو في الطعام والكسوة والدراهم والدنانير، فتفرض نفقتهم في مثل هذا المال، وإن لم يكن له مال من جنس حقهم، بل عروض وأمتعة يحتاج إلى بيعها لا تفرض نفقتهم فيه؛ لأن بيع مال الغائب لا يجوز.

قوله : عند مودع؛ أي مال له كائن مودع وهو - بفتح الدال - من أودع الغائب عنده ماله وسافر أو مديون – أي لذلك الغائب، أو مضارب - بكسر الراء - بأن أعطى الغائب ماله رجلاً مضاربة، وهي التجارة بالشركة في الربح، ويكون المال فيه من رجل والعمل من آخر، فالعامل هو المضارب.

(۱) ينظر: «درر الحكام»(١ : ٤١٧).
And he fixes maintenance for his child and his parents from his property only of the same type as their right, when it is with a depositary. And in restricting the fixing of maintenance to the wife, the parents, and the child, there is an indication that maintenance is not fixed from the absentee's property for others, such as his brother or any mahram relative except the relatives of birth. The reason for that is that their maintenance is not obligatory before a judicial decree; therefore they cannot take anything from his property if they come across it. Thus the decree in their case is a initial imposition, which is not permissible on an absent person because judgment on an absent person is not permitted in our school, as will come in its place. In contrast, the wife and the relatives of birth (children and parents) are allowed to take before a decree without his consent, so the fixing is assistance for them, not a judgment. Similarly, his slave is not fixed maintenance during his absence. So in al-Durar Sharh al-Ghurar. His statement: 'his child' means the free poor child, and similarly the ruling applies to the adult who is infirm and the poor female absolutely. His statement: 'his parents' means the poor parents who are in need of him, as will come. His statement: 'from the same type as their right' means that their right is in food, clothing, dirhams, and dinars, so their maintenance is fixed from such property. If he does not have property of that type, but goods and chattels that need to be sold, then their maintenance is not fixed therein because selling the property of an absentee is not permissible. His statement: 'when with a depositary' means that he has property that is deposited (with a depositary) – with fatha on the dal – i.e., someone with whom the absentee deposited his property and traveled, or it is a debt owed to him, or it is in the hands of a mudarib (worker in a mudaraba partnership) – with kasra on the ra' – meaning the absentee gave his property to a man as mudaraba, which is trade with profit-sharing, where the capital is from one man and the work from another, so the worker is the mudarib.
Source pages p. 554

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions under which a judge can fix maintenance for the family of an absent husband. The judge can only impose maintenance using property that is of the same kind as the maintenance right (i.e., cash, food, or clothing). If the absent husband's property consists of goods that need to be sold to provide maintenance, the judge cannot order a sale because selling an absentee's property without his permission is not allowed. Furthermore, maintenance can only be fixed for the wife, minor children, and needy parents—not for siblings or other mahram relatives whose maintenance is not already obligatory before a judicial decree. This is because for the latter, the judge would be making a new imposition, which is not permitted on an absent person. The property must be in the form of a deposit (wadiah), a debt owed to him, or a mudaraba capital. The commentator also clarifies that 'child' includes any free poor offspring, male or female, young or old if infirm, and 'parents' means poor parents dependent on him.

Key Terms
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance covering food, clothing, and shelter.
مُودَع (mūdaʿ): Depositary; one with whom the absentee left property in trust.
مَضَارِب (muḍārib): The worker in a mudaraba partnership.
غائب (ghā’ib): An absent person, one who is not present.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
From which type of property can a judge fix maintenance for the family of an absent husband?
A) Any property
B) Only property of the same kind as the maintenance right
C) Only real estate
D) Only cash

Key Takeaway
A judge can fix maintenance from an absentee's property only for wife, child, and parents, using property of the same type as maintenance right, and only if it is in a deposit, debt, or mudaraba—never by selling goods.

intermediate

Maintenance for the Absent Husband’s Wife: Evidence and Surety
Commentary on procedure for wife's maintenance when husband is absent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi procedural rule against accepting evidence of marriage for an absent husband's maintenance and evaluate Imam Zufar's dissenting position.

Core Idea
Court does not accept marriage evidence against an absent husband; Zufar permits for maintenance due to necessity.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses a critical procedural question: when a husband is absent, can his wife prove her marriage through witnesses to obtain a court order for maintenance? The Hanafi majority says no, because it would be a judgment against an absentee. But Imam Zufar disagrees, citing necessity. Let's walk through the reasoning and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the majority Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad: the court does not accept the wife's evidence of marriage to order maintenance, nor does it rule on the marriage itself.
This is the mufta bihi position from zahir al-riwayah.
2
Identify Imam Zufar's dissent
Note that Zufar holds a different view: he would accept the evidence to order maintenance (but not to rule on the marriage) and allow the wife to borrow against the husband's credit.
Zufar's view is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Examine Zufar's justification
Analyze Zufar's reasoning: husbands often abandon wives, and necessity requires accepting evidence to prevent harm. The absent husband suffers no real harm because he can recover later from wife or surety.
This is a dalil aqli (rational proof) based on maslahah.
4
Analyze the majority's response
Understand the majority's counter-argument: the general principle that judgment against an absentee is invalid is too strong to be overridden by necessity. Exceptions would open the door to abuse.
The principle 'qada' 'ala al-gha'ib la yajuz' is qati' in Hanafi usul.
5
Determine the practical difference
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf: under the majority, the wife cannot get a court order until the husband appears or his property is found. Under Zufar, she can get an immediate order and borrow.
This difference affects the wife's immediate financial security.

Book of Divorce, Chapter of Maintenance. And he makes her swear that he did not give her maintenance, not by establishing evidence of the marriage. That is, he takes a surety from them, and makes her swear that he did not give her maintenance; the pronoun in 'annahu' is a third person pronoun. (Not by establishing evidence of the marriage) meaning: the judge does not order maintenance by establishing evidence of the marriage. (And neither if he did not leave property and she established evidence of it) meaning of the marriage: so if the husband appears and confirms the woman and acknowledges her entitlement, then that; and if he denies, he recovers his property from the surety, and the surety recovers from the woman. And the same ruling applies to everyone who takes maintenance from children and parents, as in 'al-Bahr'. If the author had stated the pronoun, it would have been better, by saying: wa yakfuluhu wa yahlifuhu: he takes a surety from the taker of maintenance after his oath. (2). [1] His statement: 'surety': meaning a surety for the property, because it is restricted in the case of the absent, not for the surety. [2] His statement: 'pronoun of the absent'; this has two interpretations: the first is that the pronoun in 'annahu' is for the absent, not for the matter. The second is that the pronoun in 'annahu' refers back to the absent added to the word 'marriage' previously. [3] His statement: 'Not by establishing...' etc.; meaning if the absent has property with a trustee or someone else, and he acknowledges the property but denies the marriage, and the wife establishes evidence of her marriage to him, the judge does not order her maintenance against him by this evidence, nor does he rule on the marriage either, because evidence is not accepted when there is no present opponent, and judgment against the absent is not permitted. And the same ruling applies to every taker of inheritance who establishes evidence of his lineage to an absent person. [4] His statement: 'on the marriage'; and similarly establishing evidence of lineage for others than the wife whose maintenance is ordered from the property of the absent. [0] His statement: 'And neither...' etc.; meaning the judge does not order maintenance if the absent did not leave any property, and the wife establishes evidence that she is married so that the judge may assess her maintenance and order her to borrow upon him. And if the one who has the property does not acknowledge that and the judge does not know, and the woman wants to prove the property or the marriage or both by evidence so that judgment be given for her from the property of the absent or that she be ordered to borrow, judgment is not given for her because it is judgment against the absent. See: Ramz al-Haqa'iq (1:233). (2) 'al-Bahr al-Ra'iq' (4:214). [1] 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah 'ala Sharh al-Wiqayah. [2] And neither if he did not leave property and she established evidence of it so that the judge may order it against him and order her to borrow upon him, and he does not rule by it. And Zufar said: He rules for maintenance but not for marriage. (So that the judge may order it against him and order her to borrow upon him, and he does not rule by it) meaning the marriage, because it is judgment against the absent. And Zufar said: He rules for maintenance but not for marriage, by borrowing upon him as mentioned in the case of the maintenance of the wife of one unable to pay, because in ordering maintenance and commanding borrowing against the absent by establishing evidence, there is judgment against the absent, and acceptance of evidence without a present opponent is not permissible. Therefore, he does not rule on the marriage either. [4] [1] His statement: 'and orders her'; in the accusative, conjunction to 'orders'; meaning the woman established evidence so that the judge may order her maintenance against the absent and order her to borrow upon him due to the absence of his property so that she may recover from it. [2] His statement: 'and does not rule'; in the passive or active voice, and the pronoun refers back to the same reference as the pronoun in 'yakfuluha' and 'yahlifuha', which is the judge, and this sentence is conjoined to the sentence: 'Not by establishing evidence' meaning maintenance is not ordered by establishing evidence of the marriage, whether he left property or not, and he does not rule by that evidence for establishing the marriage. [3] His statement: 'because it'; meaning the judgment of marriage, or it is ordering maintenance and commanding borrowing. [4] His statement: 'and Zufar said'; the summary of his opinion: if she establishes evidence of the marriage so that the judge may order maintenance, when the judge does not know it or the trustee denies it, then it is incumbent upon the judge to rule by this evidence for maintenance against him; if he has property, she is given from his property, otherwise she is ordered to borrow, and he does not rule on the marriage. The reasoning: judgment on marriage is judgment against the absent, which is not permissible without necessity. As for ordering maintenance and commanding borrowing, although it is also judgment against the absent, it is often needed, because the husband frequently absents himself and leaves her without maintenance, and neither the judge nor anyone else knows of her marriage. So her evidence is accepted with a view to her right to maintenance, and there is no harm to the absent because if he appears and confirms her or she proves that in a proper way, she takes her right; otherwise, the husband recovers from the wife or from the surety whom the judge takes in such cases when ordering maintenance. Thus said al-Zayla'i in 'Sharh al-Kanz'. (1) Tabyin al-Haqa'iq (3:60).
Source pages p. 557

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the procedure for a wife seeking maintenance from an absent husband. The text is a commentary on the matn, explaining that when the husband is absent, the court takes a surety from the wife (or from the person holding the husband's property) and makes her swear that she has not been given maintenance. The court does not accept her evidence (bayyinah) to prove the marriage for the purpose of ordering maintenance, because accepting evidence without a present opponent and judging against an absentee is not permissible. The same applies even if the husband left no property; the court does not order maintenance based on her evidence alone. This is the standard Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad).

Imam Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) differs: he holds that the court should order maintenance based on her evidence, but still not rule on the marriage itself. His reasoning is necessity: husbands often abandon their wives, and the wife would be harmed if she cannot get maintenance. The harm to the absent husband is minimal because if he later confirms or she proves her case, it is fine; otherwise he can recover from the wife or the surety. The majority rejects this, maintaining the general principle that judgment against an absentee is not permitted even for maintenance.

The commentary also clarifies procedural details: if the husband returns and acknowledges the right, the matter is settled; if he denies, he recovers from the surety, who then recovers from the wife. The same rule applies to others seeking maintenance from absent persons (like parents or children). The glosses explain the pronoun references and the scope of the ruling.

Key Terms
غائب (ghā’ib): The absent husband who is not present in the jurisdiction where the wife seeks maintenance.
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance that a husband is obligated to provide to his wife, including food, clothing, and lodging.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females, used to prove a claim in court.
كفيل (kafīl): A surety who guarantees that if the wife takes maintenance and it later turns out she was not entitled, the surety will repay.
قضاء على الغائب (qaḍā’ ‘alā al-ghā’ib): A judicial ruling that affects an absent person who is not present to defend themselves. Generally not permissible in Hanafi law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the court not accept the wife's evidence of marriage to order maintenance when the husband is absent?
A) Because the wife must first get a surety
B) Because it would be a judgment against an absentee, which is not permissible
C) Because the wife must swear an oath first
D) Because marriage cannot be proven by evidence

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school generally prohibits judgment against an absentee, so a wife cannot use evidence of marriage to obtain a court order for maintenance from an absent husband, contra Zufar.

Nafaqah — Maintenance
intermediate

Father's Obligation to Maintain His Minor Child
Establishing the father's exclusive obligation for child support

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the father's obligation to maintain his minor poor child and identify the conditions that trigger or exempt this obligation.

Core Idea
A father must provide food, clothing, and housing for his minor poor child, and no one else shares this duty.

In Hanafi fiqh, the father bears the primary responsibility for the maintenance of his young children. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies that this obligation is exclusive to the father, provided the child is poor and free. It also addresses edge cases: what if the child has wealth, or the father is poor himself? Understanding these conditions is essential for correctly applying the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is the maintenance (nafaqah) of the child (tifl) by the father. The ruling is wajib (obligatory).
The child must be minor (from birth to puberty), poor, and free.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof cited is Quran 2:233, which states that the father of the child (al-mawlūd lahu) must provide provision and clothing to the mothers. This establishes the principle that maintenance obligations are specific to certain individuals.
The verse is primarily about the wife's maintenance, but used by analogy for the child.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the verse prove the father's exclusive obligation? The verse assigns maintenance of the wife solely to the husband. By analogy (qiyas), the maintenance of the child is also solely on the father, as he is the direct cause of the child's existence.
This reasoning is based on the principle that obligations are tied to specific relationships.
4
Note the exceptions and conditions
The obligation is conditional: the child must be poor (faqir) and free (not a slave). If the child is wealthy, his own property is used. The father's own poverty does not exempt him; he must earn or even beg.
If the father is insolvent and the mother is wealthy, she may spend and it becomes a debt on the father.
5
Understand the scope of maintenance
Maintenance includes three things: food, clothing, and housing. The father must provide these as long as the child is minor and poor.
Adult children are not included, except in special cases (e.g., disability) which are detailed elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في نفقة الأقارب ونفقة الطفل فقيراً على أبيه ، ولا يشركه أحد كنفقة أبويه، وعرسه [١] قوله : ونفقة الطفل ... الخ ; المراد بالنفقة ما يعم أنواعها الثلاثة : من الطعام والكسوة والسكني. والطفل يقال للولد من حين يسقط من الرحم إلى أن يحتلم، ويستوي فيه المفرد والجمع، والأنثى والذكر. وأشار به إلى أنه لا تجب على الأب نفقةُ الأولادِ البالغين إلا لعذر، وسيجيء تفصيله إن شاء الله. وقيد بالفقير; لأنه لو كان غنيّاً تجب نفقته في مالِهِ الحاضر، وإن كان عقاراً أو ثوباً أو غير ذلك، فإذا احتيج إلى النفقة كان للأب بيع . ذلك كله، والإنفاق عليه لا بد أن يقيَّد الكلام بالحرّ ; لأنَّ الطفل المملوك نفقته على مالكه لا على أبيه، حراً كان أو عبداً. كذا في «الب [رقم] [٢) قوله : على أبيه ; سواء كان غنياً أو فقيراً ، أما إن كان غنياً فيصرف عليه من ماله، وإن كان فقيرا يجب عليه أن يكتسب ويصرف على أولاده، فإن عجز عن الكسب يتكفّف ويسأل الناس كذا في «كتاب النفقات» للخصاف. وذكر في «المنية»: إنه لو كان له أب معسر وأمّ موسرة تؤمر الأم بالإنفاق، ويكون ديناً على الأب، وهي أولى من الجد الموسر. [٣]قوله : كنفقة أبويه...الخ ; يعني كما أنّ نفقة الزوجة على الزوج خاصة دون غيره ; لقوله : وَعَلَ الْمَوْلُودِ لَهُ رِزْقُهُنَّ وَكِسْوَتُهُنَّ ) (٢) ، ونفقة الأصول الفقراء على الا ﴿ أولادِ خاصة دون غيرهم من الأقارب على ما سيجيء.
Chapter on Maintenance of Relatives. And the maintenance of the child, if he is poor, is upon his father, and no one shares with him, like the maintenance of his parents and his wife. [1] His statement: 'And the maintenance of the child...' – the intended meaning of maintenance is what encompasses its three types: food, clothing, and housing. The term 'child' (ṭifl) is used for the offspring from the time it falls from the womb until it reaches puberty, and it is the same for singular and plural, female and male. He indicated by this that maintenance of adult children is not obligatory upon the father except for an excuse, and its detail will come later, God willing. He restricted it to the poor, because if he is wealthy, his maintenance is obligatory from his present wealth, even if it be real estate or clothing or otherwise; so when he needs maintenance, the father may sell all of that and spend on him. The discussion must also be restricted to a free person, because for an owned child (slave), his maintenance is upon his owner, not upon his father, whether the owner is free or slave. Thus in al-Ba[r]. [2] His statement: 'upon his father' – whether he is rich or poor. If he is rich, he spends on him from his wealth; if he is poor, it is obligatory upon him to earn and spend on his children. If he is unable to earn, he may beg and ask people. Thus in Kitāb al-Nafaqāt by al-Khaṣṣāf. And it is mentioned in al-Munyah: If there is a father who is insolvent and a mother who is wealthy, the mother is ordered to spend, and it becomes a debt upon the father, and she takes precedence over a wealthy grandfather. [3] His statement: 'like the maintenance of his parents...' – meaning just as the maintenance of the wife is specifically upon the husband to the exclusion of others, by His statement: 'And upon the father of the child is their provision and clothing,' (Quran 2:233), and the maintenance of poor ascendants is specifically upon the children to the exclusion of other relatives, as will come.
Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter on the maintenance of relatives, specifically addressing the obligation of a father to support his minor child. The base ruling states that if the child is poor, his maintenance is solely on the father, with no one else sharing this duty, analogous to the maintenance of parents and wife. The commentary expands on this: maintenance includes food, clothing, and housing. 'Child' (ṭifl) refers to offspring from birth to puberty, male or female, singular or plural. This implies that the father is not responsible for adult children except in special circumstances (e.g., disability), which will be detailed later. The condition 'poor' is crucial: if the child has wealth, his maintenance is from his own property, and the father may sell it if necessary. The discussion is also restricted to free children; the maintenance of a slave child is on his owner, not the father, regardless of the owner's status. The father's obligation holds whether he is rich or poor; if poor, he must earn or even beg to fulfill it. The commentary cites al-Khaṣṣāf and al-Munyah, noting that if the father is insolvent, the wealthy mother may be ordered to spend, and that amount becomes a debt on the father, and she takes precedence over a wealthy grandfather. The proof text for the principle of exclusive obligation is Quran 2:233, which refers to the wife's maintenance being on the husband exclusively, and similarly, ascendants' maintenance is on children exclusively.

Key Terms
الطفل (al-ṭifl): A young child from birth until puberty, for whom the father is responsible for maintenance if the child is poor.
النفقة (al-nafaqah): Maintenance consisting of food, clothing, and housing; it is obligatory for the father to provide to his minor poor child.
الفقير (al-faqīr): Poor; the child's poverty is a condition for the father's obligation of maintenance. If the child has wealth, his own property is used first.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who is obligated to provide maintenance for a minor free child who is poor?
A) The father alone, and no one shares this obligation.
B) The father and mother equally.
C) The paternal grandfather, if the father is poor.
D) The owner, if the child is a slave.

Key Takeaway
A father must provide food, clothing, and housing for his minor poor child, and no one else shares this obligation.

Nafaqah — Maintenance
intermediate
8 min
Maintenance of Minor Children on Father Exclusively
Ruling with proof on child maintenance obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on maintenance of a minor child, identify the conditions (child poor, free, minor), and explain the Quranic proof and its qiyas extension.

Core Idea
A father bears sole responsibility for his minor child’s maintenance—no one else shares this obligation.

This lesson examines the fundamental Hanafi rule that the father alone must provide maintenance (nafaqah) for his minor children. The text from al-Wiqayah and its commentary clarify the conditions, exceptions, and the Quranic basis for this obligation. Understanding this ruling is essential for family law and financial responsibilities in Islam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and ruling
The matn states: 'the maintenance of the child, [if] poor, is upon his father, and no one shares it with him.' Determine what 'child' means (tifl) and the ruling level (wajib).
Note that 'child' includes male/female, singular/plural, from birth to puberty.
2
Analyze the conditions
The ruling has two key conditions: the child must be poor (faqir) and free (hurr). If the child is wealthy, maintenance is from his own property. If the child is a slave, maintenance is on the owner.
3
Examine the father's situation
The father must provide regardless of his own wealth. If he is rich, he spends from his wealth; if poor, he must earn or even beg to support the child. An exception from al-Munya: if the father is poor and the mother wealthy, she may be ordered to spend, but it becomes a debt on the father.
4
Connect to the proof
The commentator cites Quran 2:233 which obligates the father to provide for the nursing mother. By qiyas (analogy), the father is responsible for the child's maintenance because he has legal authority (wilaya) over the child.
The proof is qat’i in transmission (Quran) but zanni in dalalah (interpretation).

Source Text

فصل في نفقة الأقارب
ونفقة الطفل فقيراً على أبيه، ولا يشركه أحد كنفقة أبويه، وعرسه.
[١] قوله: ونفقة الطفل... الخ؛ المراد بالنفقة ما يعم أنواعها الثلاثة: من الطعام والكسوة والسكنى. والطفل يقال للولد من حين يسقط من الرحم إلى أن يحتلم، ويستوي فيه المفرد والجمع، والأنثى والذكر. وأشار به إلى أنه لا تجب على الأب نفقة الأولاد البالغين إلا لعذر، وسيجيء تفصيله إن شاء الله. وقيد بالفقير؛ لأنه لو كان غنياً تجب نفقته في ماله الحاضر، وإن كان عقاراً أو ثوباً أو غير ذلك، فإذا احتيج إلى النفقة كان للأب بيع ذلك كله، والإنفاق عليه. ولا بد أن يقيَّد الكلام بالحر؛ لأن الطفل المملوك نفقته على مالكه لا على أبيه، حراً كان أو عبداً. كذا في «البحر الرائق».
[٢] قوله: على أبيه؛ سواء كان غنياً أو فقيراً، أما إن كان غنياً فيصرف عليه من ماله، وإن كان فقيراً يجب عليه أن يكتسب ويصرف على أولاده، فإن عجز عن الكسب يتكفف ويسأل الناس. كذا في «كتاب النفقات» للخصاف. وذكر في «المنية»: إنه لو كان له أب معسر وأم موسرة تؤمر الأم بالإنفاق، ويكون ديناً على الأب، وهي أولى من الجد الموسر.
[٣] قوله: كنفقة أبويه... الخ؛ يعني كما أن نفقة الزوجة على الزوج خاصة دون غيره؛ لقوله تعالى: {وعلى المولود له رزقهن وكسوتهن} (البقرة: 233)، ونفقة الأصول الفقراء على الأولاد خاصة دون غيرهم من الأقارب على ما سيجيء.
Chapter on maintenance of relatives. And the maintenance of the child, [if] poor, is upon his father, and no one shares it with him, like the maintenance of his parents and his wife. [1] His statement: 'And the maintenance of the child ... etc.': What is meant by maintenance is what encompasses its three types: food, clothing, and lodging. The term 'child' is applied to the offspring from the time it falls from the womb until it reaches puberty, and it is the same for singular and plural, female and male. And he indicated by it that the maintenance of adult children is not obligatory upon the father except for an excuse, and its detail will come, God willing. And he restricted it to 'poor'; because if he is wealthy, his maintenance is obligatory from his present wealth, even if it is real estate or clothing or otherwise; if there is a need for expenditure, the father may sell all of that and spend on him. And the statement must be restricted to the free child, because the maintenance of an owned child is upon his owner, not his father, whether the owner is free or a slave. Thus in 'al-Bahr al-Raiq'. [2] His statement: 'upon his father': whether he is rich or poor. If he is rich, he spends from his wealth; if he is poor, he must earn and spend on his children. If he is unable to earn, he begs and asks people. Thus in 'Kitab al-Nafaqat' by al-Khassaf. And it is mentioned in 'al-Munya': If he has a poor father and a wealthy mother, the mother is ordered to spend, and it becomes a debt upon the father, and she has precedence over the wealthy grandfather. [3] His statement: 'like the maintenance of his parents ... etc.': Meaning that just as the maintenance of the wife is upon the husband exclusively, due to His statement: {And upon the father of the child is their provision and clothing}, and the maintenance of poor ascendants is upon the children exclusively, as will come.
Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the chapter on maintenance of relatives. The matn states that the maintenance (nafaqah) of a minor child, if the child is poor, is solely upon the father, and no one shares this responsibility with him, just as the father alone is responsible for the maintenance of his parents and his wife. The commentator clarifies that 'nafaqah' includes food, clothing, and lodging. The term 'child' (tifl) applies from birth to puberty, covering singular/plural and male/female. The condition 'poor' is significant: if the child has wealth, his own property is used for his maintenance, even if it is illiquid assets like real estate—the father may sell it to provide for him. Also, the child must be free; a slave child's maintenance is upon the owner. The father must maintain the child whether he is rich or poor; if the father is poor, he must earn, and if unable, he may beg. A noteworthy detail from the commentary: if the father is poor and the mother wealthy, she may be ordered to spend, but it becomes a debt on the father, and she has priority over the grandfather. The proof cited is the Quranic verse about maintaining wives (2:233), extended by analogy to children and parents.

Key Terms
النفقة (al-nafaqah): Maintenance including food, clothing, and lodging.
الطفل (al-tifl): A child from birth until puberty (ihtilam).
الفقير (al-faqir): One who has no wealth or income sufficient for maintenance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, who is responsible for the maintenance of a poor minor child?
A) The mother exclusively
B) The father exclusively
C) Both parents equally
D) The paternal grandfather

Key Takeaway
The father bears the sole obligation to maintain his poor, free, minor child, and no other relative is obligated to share this duty.

Mother's Obligation to Breastfeed and Father's Duty
Explains the ruling that the mother is not obligated to breastfeed except when she is the only option.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on maternal breastfeeding obligation and apply the distinction between legal (qada') and religious (dinaya) duty.

Core Idea
Mother not legally obliged to breastfeed unless she is the only option; father must provide a wet nurse.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies a common misconception: the mother is not automatically required to breastfeed. Instead, the obligation shifts based on necessity. We will explore the legal categories, the father's responsibility, and the boundaries of hiring the mother herself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary legal ruling
Read the matn: 'his mother is not obliged to breastfeed him except when she is the only option.' This is the foundational principle.
2
Distinguish between qada' and dinaya
The commentary explains that 'legally' (qada'an) she is not obliged, but 'religiously' (diyanatan) it is recommended. Note when the legal obligation activates.
Obligation becomes qada' only when she is the sole available wet nurse.
3
Understand the father's duty
If the mother does not breastfeed and is not the only option, the father must hire a wet nurse. The wet nurse must breastfeed at the mother's home due to her custody rights.
The phrase 'at her place' is critical.
4
Analyze the exception: hiring the mother
The text states it is invalid to hire the mother if she is married to him or in 'idda from a raj'i divorce. The same applies to a ba'in divorce. Understand why this is invalid.
Reasons: conflict of marital duties or already obligated by custody.
5
Connect to broader principle of nafaqah
The commentary ends by stating that maintenance of young children is exclusively on the father, citing zakat al-fitr as evidence. This reinforces the father's financial responsibility.
The father's obligation includes breastfeeding costs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس على أمه إرضاعه إلا إذا تعينت، ويستأجر الأب من ترضعه عندها، ولو استأجرها منكوحة أو معتدة من رجعي لترضعه لم يجز، وفي المبتوتة كذلك.
[١] قوله: ليس.... الخ؛ أي لا يجب على أم الولد سواء كانت في نكاح أبيه أو مطلقة أن ترضع الصبي، وهذا قضاء؛ وذلك لأن كفاية الولد واجبة على الوالد. ومنها: أجرة الرضاعة، فيجب عليه أن يستأجر من ترضعه، ولا يجب على الأم إرضاعه، وأما ديانة فيجب عليها إرضاعه، وأما إذا تعينت الأم لإرضاعه، فحينئذ يجب عليها إرضاعه قضاء أيضاً؛ صيانة للصبي.
[٢] قوله: بأن لا يوجد؛ أي لا توجد مرضعة ترضع الصبي بإجارة أو بغير إجارة، أو وجدت مرضعة، ولكن الصبي لا يشرب لبن غير الأم، فحينئذ تتعين الأم للإرضاع، ويجب عليه ذلك.
[٣] قوله: ويستأجر؛ أي إذا لم تتعين الأم للرضاعة ولم ترضعه يجب على الأب أن يستأجر امرأة ترضعه عند الأم، وإنما قيد بقوله: عندها؛ لأن حق الحضانة للأم، فلا يجوز للأب أن يخرجه من يدها ويعطيه المرضعة لترضعه في بيت آخر.
[٤] قوله: منكوحة؛ حال من ضمير المفعول الراجع إلى الأم، وحاصله: إنه لا يجوز له استئجار أم الصبي لإرضاعه إذا كانت منكوحة له أو معتدة من طلاقه الرجعي.
كذلك تكون نفقة الأولاد الصغار على الأب خاصة دون الأم ودون غيرها من الأقارب؛ لأن الأب له ولاية ومؤنة على الصغير، ولذلك وجبت صدقة الفطر عن الصغير على الأب خاصة، كما ثبت ذلك بالسنن المروية في الصحاح والسنن.
And his mother is not obliged to breastfeed him except when she is the only option. And the father hires someone to breastfeed him at her place. And if he hires her while she is married to him, or in 'idda from a raj'i divorce to breastfeed him, it is not valid, and similarly in the case of a ba'in divorce. [1] His statement: 'is not ... etc.': Meaning that the mother of the child, whether she is in the marriage of his father or divorced, is not legally obliged to breastfeed the baby. This is a legal ruling; because the maintenance of the child is obligatory upon the father. And part of that is the fee for breastfeeding, so he must hire someone to breastfeed him, and the mother is not obliged to breastfeed him. However, from a religious perspective, she should breastfeed him. And if the mother becomes the only option for breastfeeding, then at that point it becomes legally obligatory for her to breastfeed him, for the protection of the child. [2] His statement: 'when she is the only option': i.e., when there is no wet nurse who will breastfeed the child for hire or for free, or there is a wet nurse but the child does not drink the milk of anyone other than the mother, then the mother becomes the only option for breastfeeding, and she must do it. [3] His statement: 'and the father hires': i.e., if the mother is not the only option for breastfeeding and she does not breastfeed, then the father must hire a woman to breastfeed the child at the mother's place. He specifically said 'at her place' because the right of custody belongs to the mother, so the father is not allowed to take the child from her hand and give him to a wet nurse to breastfeed in another house. [4] His statement: 'married': is a circumstantial state referring to the object pronoun referring to the mother. Its summary: It is not permissible for him to hire the mother of the child to breastfeed him if she is married to him or in 'idda from a raj'i divorce. Similarly, the maintenance of young children is exclusively upon the father, not upon the mother or any other relative, because the father has guardianship and responsibility for the young child. Therefore, zakat al-fitr for the young child is specifically upon the father, as established by the transmitted sunnah in the authentic collections.
Source pages p. 564

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal ruling regarding breastfeeding. The matn states that the mother is not legally obligated to breastfeed her child unless she is the only available wet nurse. The father must hire a wet nurse to breastfeed at the mother's home. It is invalid to hire the mother herself if she is his wife or in 'idda from a raj'i divorce; the same applies to a ba'in divorce. The commentator explains that 'legally' (qada'an) the mother is not obliged, but 'religiously' (diyanatan) she should. The obligation shifts to legal only if she is the sole option—when no other wet nurse exists (for hire or free) or the child refuses milk other than hers. The father must hire a wet nurse to come to the mother's house because the mother has custody (hadana) rights; he cannot take the child to another house. Hiring the mother herself is invalid because it leads to a conflict of marital duties (in the case of a current marriage or raj'i 'idda) or because she is already responsible for the child in a ba'in divorce. The passage concludes by reiterating that maintenance of minor children is exclusively on the father, citing the obligation of zakat al-fitr as evidence.

Key Terms
الإرضاع (al-irda'): Breastfeeding of a child by a wet nurse or mother.
المبتوتة (al-mabtutah): A woman divorced by a ba'in (irrevocable) divorce.
الرجعي (al-raj'i): A revocable divorce where the husband may take back his wife during the 'idda without a new contract.
الحضانة (al-hadana): Custody of a young child, which remains with the mother after divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Only when she is the only available wet nurse
C) Always, regardless of circumstances
D) When the child is older than two years

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a mother is not legally bound to breastfeed unless she is the only available wet nurse; otherwise, the father must provide a wet nurse at the mother's home.
